Prophethood
beginner
4 min
The Philosophers' Denial of Prophethood
First group: philosophers denying free will and particular knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first group of deniers of prophethood—the philosophers—reject prophethood by denying God's free agency and knowledge of particulars.

Core Idea
Denial of prophethood can stem from denying God's free will and particular knowledge.

The author begins his classification by identifying the first group of deniers, who are the philosophers. They hold that God is necessary by essence, not a free agent, and that He does not know particulars. From this, they conclude that prophethood is impossible. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the first group
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to get the meaning.
Notice the phrase 'طريقة الفلاسفة' – the method of the philosophers.
2
Identify the two denied attributes
The group denies that God is a free agent (فاعل مختار) and that He knows particulars (عالم بالجزئيات).
These two denials are key to their rejection of prophethood.
3
Understand the consequence
Because of these denials, the door to proving prophethood is closed. Ask yourself why prophethood would require free agency and particular knowledge.
Prophethood involves God choosing to send messengers and knowing specific human conditions.
4
Recognize the source
The author identifies this view as the method of the philosophers. This helps situate the argument in a historical debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأمل في شرح مخاحب الناس في هذا الباب اعلم أن منكري النبوات فرق: الفرقة الأولى: الذين قالوا: إله العالم موجب بالذات لا فاعل بالاختيار. فإن كل من أنكر كونه تعالى فاعلاً مختاراً، وأنكر كونه عالماً بالجزئيات. فقد انسد عليه باب إثبات النبوات وهذه الطريقة هي طريقة الفلاسفة. والفرقة الثانية: الذين سلّموا أن إله العالم فاعل مختار ولكنهم أنكروا الأمر والنهي والتكليف. قالوا: وإذ ثبت هذا فقد بطل القول بالنبوة. لأن الأنبياء عليهم السلام إنما جاءوا بتقرير التكاليف. ولما كان القول بالتكليف عندهم باطلاً، فقد بطل الأصل وعند بطلان الأصل يكون الفرع أولى بالبطلان. والفرقة الثالثة: الذين سلموا أن إله العالم أمر عباده بأشياء، ونهاهم عن
The first chapter on explaining the doctrines of people in this topic. Know that the deniers of prophethood are groups: The first group: those who said: The God of the world is necessary by essence, not an agent by choice. For whoever denies that He, exalted, is a free agent, and denies that He knows particulars, the door to proving prophethood is closed before him, and this method is the method of the philosophers. The second group: those who conceded that the God of the world is a free agent, but they denied command, prohibition, and obligation. They said: Since this is established, the statement about prophethood is invalid. Because the prophets, peace be upon them, only came to establish obligations. And since the statement about obligation is invalid in their view, the root is invalid, and when the root is invalid, the branch is more deserving of invalidity. And the third group: those who conceded that the God of the world commanded His servants with things and prohibited them from...
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a classification of deniers of prophethood into three groups. The author first states that the deniers of prophethood consist of several factions, then enumerates them. The first group are those who hold that God is necessary by His essence (wājib bil-dhāt) and not a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār). They also deny that God knows particulars. Consequently, any such person has blocked the possibility of proving prophethood. The author identifies this as the position of the philosophers. The second group concedes that God is a free agent but denies that He issues commands, prohibitions, or obligations (taklīf). They argue that if obligation is negated, then prophethood is also negated, because prophets only came to establish obligations. Since the root (obligation) is invalid in their view, the branch (prophethood) is even more deserving of invalidity. The third group begins by acknowledging that God commanded His servants with things and prohibited them, but the passage is cut off before completing their position. This enumeration shows that the denial of prophethood stems from deeper theological stances regarding divine agency, knowledge, and obligation.

Key Terms
منكري النبوات
إله العالم
موجب بالذات
فاعل مختار
الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Deniers of prophethood are only those who reject God's existence.
Correction: This passage shows that even some who affirm God's existence (philosophers) can deny prophethood by denying His free agency and particular knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the first group, what two divine attributes do they deny?
A) Divine power and will
B) Free agency and knowledge of particulars
C) Mercy and justice
D) Speech and eternity

Key Takeaway
The first group of deniers, the philosophers, reject prophethood because they deny that God is a free agent and knows particulars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever mentally distance God from your daily affairs, treating Him like a distant 'necessary being' rather than a caring Lord Who knows every detail of your life? What would it mean for you to truly believe He knows your struggles—and still chooses to act?
?
If you were to adopt the philosophers' view of God (as uninvolved and unknowing of particulars), how would your prayer life change? Would you still cry out to Him as Someone who hears and responds?
?
This passage says that denying God's free choice and particular knowledge 'closes the door' to believing in prophethood. Is there any area of your life where you functionally deny God's involvement—acting as if He doesn't see or care? What would it take to open that door again?

Prophethood

Denial of Prophethood Through Denial of Obligation
Second group: those who affirm free agency but deny obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second group of deniers concedes God's free agency but denies obligation, thus concluding that prophethood is invalid.

Core Idea
Denying divine obligation makes prophethood unnecessary.

The second group takes a different approach: they affirm that God is a free agent, but they deny that He issues commands, prohibitions, and obligations. Since prophets came to establish obligations, denying obligation undermines the entire purpose of prophethood, making it invalid. Let's examine their argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second group
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note that they concede free agency but deny taklīf.
Taklīf means religious obligation.
2
Trace the logical argument
They say: Since there is no obligation, then prophethood is invalid because prophets only came to convey obligations.
The argument uses a root-branch analogy.
3
Understand the root-branch analogy
Obligation is the root (أصل), prophethood is the branch (فرع). If the root is invalid, the branch is even more invalid.
This is a common logical structure in Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the implication
This shows that accepting God as a free agent is not enough; one must also accept that God imposes obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

For whoever denies that He, exalted, is a free agent, and denies that He knows particulars, the door to proving prophethood is closed before him, and this method is the method of the philosophers. Commentary

The first group are those who hold that God is necessary by His essence (wājib bil-dhāt) and not a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār). They also deny that God knows particulars. Key Terms

فرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If someone believes God is a free agent, they must accept prophethood.
Correction: This group shows that even believing in free agency does not automatically lead to accepting prophethood if one denies that God imposes obligations.

Checkpoint
According to the second group, what is the relationship between obligation and prophethood?
A) Obligation is a result of prophethood
B) Prophethood is the root, obligation is the branch
C) Obligation is the root, prophethood is the branch
D) They are unrelated

Key Takeaway
The second group argues that since prophets only exist to convey obligations, if there are no obligations, then prophethood is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified neglecting a religious obligation by convincing yourself that God's mercy or freedom means He doesn't truly require anything from you? How does the argument in this lesson expose the flaw in that reasoning?
?
If you believe in prophethood, do you truly accept that every prophet came to convey binding commands from God? What command of the Prophet ﷺ do you find hardest to follow, and what does your resistance reveal about your view of obligation?
?
Imagine you denied obligation – would you still have any reason to follow the Prophet? This lesson says no. Does your daily life reflect that you see prophethood as inseparable from obligation, or do you treat some commands as optional?

Prophethood
beginner
4 min
The Philosophers' Denial: Free Agency and Knowledge of Particulars
Classification of the first group of deniers of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first group of deniers of prophethood (philosophers) and why their denial of God's free agency and knowledge of particulars blocks the proof of prophethood.

Core Idea
Denying God's free choice and knowledge of particulars shuts the door to prophethood.

This passage introduces the first sect that denies prophethood: the philosophers. They claim God's actions are necessary, not free, and that He does not know particular events. The author shows how these two denials logically prevent any proof of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the concise classification.
The passage uses the phrase 'اعلم أن' (know that) to signal important information.
2
Identify the two denials
Find the two specific denials the philosophers make. They are: denying that God is a free agent, and denying that He knows particulars.
These two denials are linked by 'وأنكر' (and denies).
3
Reflect on the consequence
Consider why these denials 'close the door' to proving prophethood. Prophethood requires a God who freely chooses to send messengers and knows the individuals He sends them to.
The author states this consequence clearly: 'فقد انسد عليه باب إثبات النبوات'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن منكري النبوات فرق: الفرقة الأولى: الذين قالوا: إله العالم موجب بالذات لا فاعل بالاختيار. فإن كل من أنكر كونه تعالى فاعلاً مختاراً، وأنكر كونه عالماً بالجزئيات، فقد انسد عليه باب إثبات النبوات وهذه الطريقة هي طريقة الفلاسفة.
Know that the deniers of prophethood are sects: The first sect: those who said: The God of the world is necessitating by essence, not a free agent. For whoever denies that He – exalted – is a free agent, and denies that He knows particulars, the door of proving prophethood is closed to him, and this way is the way of the philosophers.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage classifies the first group of deniers of prophethood: the philosophers. The author states that the deniers of prophethood are divided into sects. The first sect holds that God is necessary in essence (موجب بالذات), meaning His actions are necessary consequences of His nature, not free choices. They also deny that God knows particular events. The author argues that these two denials—denying free agency and denying knowledge of particulars—automatically close the door to proving prophethood. The reason, though not expanded here, is that prophethood requires a free agent who chooses to send messengers and knows particular individuals and circumstances. This path is identified as the way of the philosophers, a clear reference to the peripatetic Aristotelian tradition.

Key Terms
منكري النبوات
موجب بالذات
فاعل مختار
الجزئيات
الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'موجب بالذات' means God has no will at all, but it means His actions follow necessarily from His essence, not that He is compelled by an external force.
Correction: In Islamic philosophy, 'موجب بالذات' describes God as a necessary being whose actions are not arbitrary but flow from His nature, yet this still negates free choice in the sense of deliberative will.

Checkpoint
What two things do the first group of deniers of prophethood deny?
A) God's existence and unity
B) God's free agency and knowledge of particulars
C) God's power and will
D) God's mercy and justice

Key Takeaway
The first sect denying prophethood are philosophers who hold that God's actions are necessary and He does not know particulars, thereby making prophethood impossible to prove.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted as if God's forgiveness is automatic—as if He must forgive you because you performed certain deeds? How does the reality of God being a free agent (فاعل مختار) challenge that comfortable assumption? This week, when you ask for forgiveness, do it with the awareness that He chooses to forgive you—not because He has to.
?
If God did not know the specific details of your life—your struggles, your hidden sins, your unique circumstances—could He send you a prophet tailored to your need? Yet He knows every particular. Does that knowledge humble you? Take a moment to thank Him for knowing you completely.
?
The philosophers said God cannot send prophets because He does not choose freely and does not know details. By contrast, your faith rests on a God who both chooses and knows. Are you living as if that matters? Or have you reduced prophethood to a mere historical fact? Commit to reading one prophetic narration this week with the conviction that it was sent specifically for you.

The Second Denying Sect: Denying Divine Commands and Obligations
Classification of the second group: they admit God as free agent but deny commands, prohibitions, and obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument of those who accept God's free agency but deny divine obligations, and why they conclude that prophethood is invalid.

Core Idea
Rejecting divine obligation makes prophethood impossible.

In the previous lesson, we met a group that denied God's free agency. Now we encounter a second group that concedes God's free agency but denies that He issues commands, prohibitions, or imposes obligations. They argue that since prophethood is founded on delivering obligations, denying obligation necessarily entails denying prophethood. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow.
Notice the logical structure: concession → denial → implication → conclusion.
2
Identify what they accept and deny
The passage clearly states they accept God as a free agent (فاعل مختار) but deny commands (الأمر), prohibitions (النهي), and obligations (التكليف). Mark these two parts.
This distinguishes them from the first sect who denied free agency.
3
Trace the logical argument
They argue: prophethood is only for establishing obligations; obligation is invalid; therefore prophethood is invalid. The author uses a 'root and branch' analogy: obligation is the root, prophethood is the branch.
Understand why they see prophethood as dependent on obligation.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask yourself: why does denying obligation lead to denying prophethood? What does this imply about the necessity of divine commands for prophethood?
This argument is a reductio ad absurdum against those who accept God's will but reject His law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرقة الثانية: الذين سلموا أن إله العالم فاعل مختار ولكنهم أنكروا الأمر والنهي والتكليف. قالوا: وإذ ثبت هذا فقد بطل القول بالنبوة. لأن الأنبياء عليهم السلام إنما جاءوا بتقرير التكاليف. ولما كان القول بالتكليف عندهم باطلاً، فقد بطل الأصل وعند بطلان الأصل يكون الفرع أولى بالبطلان.
And the second sect: those who conceded that the God of the world is a free agent but they denied command, prohibition, and obligation. They said: Since this is established, the statement of prophethood is invalidated. Because the prophets, peace be upon them, only came to establish obligations. And since the statement of obligation is in their view invalid, then the root is invalidated, and when the root is invalid, the branch is more deserving of invalidation.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents the second group of deniers of prophethood. Unlike the first group, they accept that God is a free agent (فاعل مختار). However, they deny that God issues commands (الأمر), prohibitions (النهي), or imposes obligations (التكليف). Their argument is that prophethood is solely about delivering obligations; if obligation itself is invalid, then the entire institution of prophethood, which is based on obligation, becomes invalid. The author uses a logical structure: the root (الأصل) is the obligation, and the branch (الفرع) is prophethood; when the root is invalid, the branch is even more so invalid. This is a reductio ad absurdum against those who accept divine free agency but reject moral-legal obligations.

Key Terms
الأمر
النهي
التكليف
الأصل
الفرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying divine obligation is equivalent to atheism.
Correction: This group specifically affirms God's free agency but rejects obligation; it is a distinct theological position that leads to rejection of prophethood, not a denial of God's existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the second group of deniers consider prophethood invalid?
A) Because miracles are impossible
B) Because God cannot send messengers
C) Because obligation is invalid, and prophethood is its branch
D) Because reason alone can know all obligations

Key Takeaway
Denying divine obligation destroys the very basis for prophethood and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself accepting that God exists and has power, yet resisting His specific commands as if they are optional or outdated? How does this passage expose the inconsistency of that mindset?
?
If you truly believed that prophethood has no validity unless it delivers binding obligations, would you still treat the words of the Prophet ﷺ as merely advisory? What would you need to change starting today?
?
When you read the phrase 'the root is invalid, the branch is more deserving of invalidation', does any area of your life come to mind where you have cut the root of obligation while trying to keep the branch of faith? What will you do to restore the root?

The Third Denying Sect: Rationalists Who Deem Prophets Unnecessary
Classification of the third group: they accept commands but claim reason suffices, making prophets redundant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rationalist position that claims intellect alone is sufficient for knowing divine obligations, rendering prophethood redundant.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot replace prophecy because it cannot fully know divine commands.

This lesson examines the third group of deniers of prophethood—those who accept God's commands but argue that human intellect alone can discern them, thus rendering prophets unnecessary. We'll explore this claim and its implications for the necessity of divine revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the group concedes the reality of divine commands but then limits their knowability.
Pay attention to the word 'إلا' which signals the twist in their argument.
2
Identify the concession and the claim
The group concedes that God commands and prohibits. Their claim is that intellect alone suffices. This is the core of their rationalist position.
3
Reflect on the consequence
The author concludes that if intellect suffices, then sending prophets has no benefit. Ask yourself: Is this conclusion valid?
This anticipates Islamic critiques of pure rationalism.
4
Consider the implicit refutation
The passage implicitly refutes this by highlighting the absurd consequence. How might a defender of prophethood respond?
Think about the limitations of intellect mentioned elsewhere in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرقة الثالثة: الذين سلموا أن إله العالم أمر عباده بأشياء، ونهاهم عن أشياء إلا انهم قالوا: العقول وحدها كافية في معرفة تلك التكاليف. فلم يكن في بعثة الأنبياء عليهم السلام فائدة.
And the third sect: those who conceded that the God of the world commanded His servants with things and prohibited them from things, except that they said: intellects alone are sufficient for knowing those obligations. So there was no benefit in sending the prophets, peace be upon them.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents the third group of deniers. They go further than the second group: they accept that God issues commands and prohibitions. However, they claim that human intellect (العقول) alone is sufficient to know these obligations. Since the purpose of prophets is to convey obligations, if intellect can independently arrive at them, then prophethood becomes superfluous. The author counters this implicitly by stating that this leads to the conclusion that sending prophets was of no benefit. This group represents a rationalist position that denies the need for revelation, anticipating debates about the sufficiency of reason in theology and ethics.

Key Terms
العقول
التكاليف
بعثة الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this group rejects prophecy entirely, but they actually accept God's commands while denying the necessity of prophetic transmission.
Correction: The passage shows they affirm God's commands and prohibitions; they only deny that prophets are needed to convey them.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the third group gives for prophethood being unnecessary?
A) Miracles are impossible
B) Intellect alone suffices to know obligations
C) God does not command anything
D) Prophets were not sent to all nations

Key Takeaway
The third group denied the need for prophets by claiming that human reason can independently know all divine obligations, thus making prophethood superfluous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area of your life where you have effectively replaced the Prophet's guidance with your own reasoning? What would it cost you to surrender that to Allah's command?
?
When was the last time you felt that following a specific teaching of the Prophet was unnecessary or outdated? What does that reveal about how much you truly affirm prophethood?
?
If you were to ask Allah tonight: 'Why did You send prophets when my intellect could figure things out?'—what would your heart already know the answer is, and what would it cost you to live by it starting tomorrow?

Prophethood

Fourth Group's Attack on Miracles
Fourth group denies miracle-based proof of prophethood and lists five sub-arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth group's argument that miracles fail to prove prophethood, along with their five sub-arguments attacking the probative value of miracles.

Core Idea
Miracles are rejected not because they are impossible, but because they fail to guarantee truthfulness.

This passage presents the fourth major group of deniers of prophethood. Unlike previous groups, they accept that knowledge of prophethood comes only through miracles—but then argue that miracles have no evidentiary value. The author systematically dismantles the miracle proof by listing five sub-arguments, each attacking a different assumption. This lesson examines their core claim and the five sub-arguments, showing how they challenge the standard proof from miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the core claim
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the fourth group states their position: miracles have no indication of truthfulness, so prophethood is invalid.
Notice that they accept miracles as the only means but deny their value.
2
Identify the five sub-arguments
The passage lists five sub-sects (وجوه) that attack miracles. As you read, note each one's main point: denial of possibility, unknown doer, no proof of truthfulness, jabr argument, and historical transmission issues.
The fifth sub-sect is incomplete in the source.
3
Understand the first three sub-arguments
The first sub-sect denies miracles are possible. The second admits possibility but says we cannot attribute them to God. The third admits divine causation but denies they prove truthfulness.
These build on each other: from impossibility to lack of evidence.
4
Analyze the fourth sub-argument (jabr)
The fourth sub-sect uses the doctrine of divine compulsion to argue that God can affirm a liar, so a miracle doesn't guarantee truthfulness.
This is a theological argument based on predestination.
5
Review the incomplete fifth sub-argument
The fifth sub-sect questions the historical transmission of miracles, saying we only have reports, not direct observation.
Though incomplete, it raises the issue of historical certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الرابعة: الذين قالوا: لا طريق لنا إلى معرفة نبوة الأنبياء، إلا بواسطة المعجزات، والمعجزات لا دلالة فيها البتة على صدق الأنبياء، وإذا لم يوجد شيء يدل على النبوة إلا المعجز، وثبت أن المعجز لا يدل البتة على صدق النبي، فحينئذ يلزم فساد القول بالنبوة. ثم إن القائلين بهذا القول ذكروا في الطعن في المعجزات وجوهاً كثيرة. فالفرقة الأولى: الذين أنكروا وجود المعجزات، قالوا: خرق العادات ممتنع في العقول. والفرقة الثانية: الذين سلموا إمكان انخراق العادات، إلا أنهم قالوا: لم يوجد دليل يدل على أن الفاعل لتلك الأفعال الخارقة للعادات، هو الله سبحانه. والفرقة الثالثة: الذين قالوا: هب أن الدليل دل على أن فاعل تلك الخوارق هو الله تعالى، إلا أنها – مع تسليم هذا الأصل – لا تدل على صدق المدعي للرسالة. والفرقة الرابعة: الذين قالوا: هب أن المعجز يدل على أن الله تعالى صدق ذلك المدعي في دعواه، لكن تصديق الله إياه في دعواه لا يدل على كونه صادقاً، بدليل: أن القول بالجبر حق، ومتى كان كذلك وجب القطع بأن فاعل القبائح بأسرها هو الله، ومتى كان كذلك امتنع أن يقال: إن الفعل الفلاني: قبيح، فيمتنع من الله تعالى فعله، وإذا كان كذلك، لم يقبح من الله تعالى: تصديق الكاذب. والفرقة الخامسة من الطاعنين في المعجزات: الذين قالوا: هب أن المعجزات تدل على صدق المدعي، إلا أنا ما شاهدنا ظهور تلك المعجزات، وإنما الغاية القصوى: أن الناس أخبروا: أنها وقعت في الأزمنة الماضية، إلا
The fourth sect: those who said: There is no way for us to know prophethood except by means of miracles, and miracles have no indication whatsoever of the truthfulness of the prophets. And if nothing exists that indicates prophethood except miracles, and it is established that the miracle does not indicate at all the truthfulness of the prophet, then consequently the statement of prophethood is invalidated. Then those who said this statement mentioned many arguments against miracles. So the first sub-sect: those who denied the existence of miracles; they said: violation of habits is inconceivable in intellects. And the second sub-sect: those who conceded the possibility of violation of habits, except that they said: no proof exists indicating that the doer of those unusual acts is Allah, exalted. And the third sub-sect: those who said: Grant that proof indicates that the doer of those extraordinary phenomena is Allah, exalted, yet – with the admission of this principle – they do not indicate the truthfulness of the claimant of messengership. And the fourth sub-sect: those who said: Grant that the miracle indicates that Allah, exalted, has affirmed that claimant in his claim, but Allah's affirming him in his claim does not indicate that he is truthful, by evidence: that the doctrine of compulsion (jabr) is true, and when it is so, it is necessarily concluded that the doer of all evil deeds is Allah, and when it is so, it becomes impossible to say: such and such act is evil, so it is impossible for Allah to do it, and when it is so, it is not evil for Allah to affirm the liar. And the fifth sub-sect of those who attack miracles: those who said: Grant that miracles indicate the truthfulness of the claimant, except that we have not witnessed the appearance of those miracles, and the utmost is that people reported that they occurred in past times, except...
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth major group of deniers of prophethood. Unlike previous groups that reject the basis of prophethood, this group accepts that knowledge of prophethood comes only through miracles, but they attack the evidentiary value of miracles. The author first states their core claim: miracles have no indication of prophetic truthfulness. Since the only means to prove prophethood is miracles, and they fail, prophethood is invalid. Then the author lists five sub-arguments (وجوه) that these deniers use against miracles. The first sub-sect denies the very possibility of miracles, calling violations of habits (خرق العادات) rationally impossible. The second admits possibility but says we cannot attribute these acts to God. The third admits divine causation but denies that miracles prove the claimant's truthfulness. The fourth uses the doctrine of divine compulsion (الجبر) to argue that affirming a liar is not evil for God, so the miracle does not guarantee truthfulness. The fifth, incomplete here, admits miracles indicate truth but questions the historical transmission of miracle accounts. This is a systematic demolition of the standard proof from miracles, anticipating classical responses.

Key Terms
المعجزات
خرق العادات
الجبر
تصديق الله
فاعل القبائح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth group rejects the existence of miracles entirely.
Correction: In fact, they accept miracles as a concept but argue that they do not provide proof of prophetic truthfulness.

Checkpoint
What is the core claim of the fourth group of deniers of prophethood?
A) Miracles are impossible
B) Miracles have no indication of prophetic truthfulness
C) God does not exist
D) Reason is sufficient without prophets

Key Takeaway
The fourth group does not reject miracles outright but systematically attacks their ability to prove prophetic truthfulness, using five distinct arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read or hear about the miracles of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), do you accept them with certainty? Or, honestly, is there a part of you that questions them because you did not witness them? How do you respond to that doubt right now, knowing the fifth sub-sect's objection?
?
The fourth sub-sect argued that since God is the creator of all actions, He could affirm a liar. Have you ever considered that this logical possibility could undermine your trust in prophetic miracles? What in your faith reassures you that God would not do that?
?
If you were to defend the probative value of miracles against these five arguments, which one would you find most challenging to answer? How does your current knowledge equip you to reply?

The Epistemological Foundation and the Five Sects
Author critiques reliance on probabilistic reports and identifies five sects attacking miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects reports (khabar) as a basis for proving prophethood and which five sects attack prophethood via miracles.

Core Idea
Certain matters require certain proofs; reports only yield probability.

This lesson begins by establishing a key epistemological principle: reports (khabar) do not produce certain knowledge (al-ilm al-yaqini), only probability (zann). Since prophethood is a matter of certainty, basing its proof on probabilistic reports is invalid. The author then identifies the first five sects that attack prophethood by attacking miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on reports and certainty
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the author's logical flow: statements on the nature of reports, then the conclusion that building proofs on probability is false.
2
Identify the five sects
The passage mentions 'these five sects are the ones who attack prophethood through attacking miracles.' Reflect on why miracles are central to proving prophethood.
The author does not name the five sects here; they are enumerated later.
3
Understand the epistemological principle
Ask yourself: Why does the author insist on certain knowledge for prophethood? How does this principle affect the validity of the deniers' arguments?
This principle is foundational for all subsequent discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن الخبر لا يفيد العلم اليقيني . وإنما القدر الممكن فيه : حصول الظن . لأن هذه المسألة مسألة يقينية . فبناء إثباتها على الطريق الذي لا يفيد إلا الظن : يكون باطلاً فاسداً . فهذه الفرق الخمس هم الذين يطعنون في النبوات بواسطة الطعن في المعجزات .
وأما الفرقة الخامسة من الطاعنين في النبوات : الذين قالوا : إنه نقل عنهم أحوال لا يليق بالمحققين الاشتغال بها ، وذلك يوجب الطعن في كونهم رسلا من عند الله تعالى
والفرقة السادسة : الذين قالوا : لو أراد الله تعالى إرسال الرسل ، لكان يجب أن يكون رسولا من الملائكة ، وأن يظهر عليه معجزات قاهرة متوالية وهذا القول حكاه الله تعالى في القرآن مراراً متوالية كثيرة .
فهذا هو الإشارة إلى ضبط فرق المنكرين للنبوات . أما الكلام في أن إله العالم فاعل مختار ، لا موجب بالذات . فقد سبق على الاستقصاء . فلا فائدة في الإعادة
وأما سائر المذاهب فإنا نعقد في كل واحد منها فصلاً مفرداً . والله أعلم
That report does not yield certain knowledge. Rather the possible extent in it is the occurrence of probability. Because this issue is a certain issue. So building its proof upon a method that does not yield except probability is false and corrupt. So these five sects are the ones who attack prophethood through attacking miracles.
As for the fifth sect among the attackers of prophethood: they said that there have been transmitted about them circumstances that are not befitting for investigators to occupy themselves with, and that necessitates attacking them being messengers from God Almighty.
And the sixth sect: they said that if God Almighty had intended to send messengers, then it would have been necessary that a messenger be from the angels, and that overpowering consecutive miracles appear upon him. And this saying God Almighty has narrated in the Qur'an many consecutive times.
So this is the indication of the enumeration of the sects denying prophethood. As for the discourse concerning that the God of the world is a free agent, not a necessary agent by essence, it has already preceded in full detail. So there is no benefit in repetition.
And as for the remaining schools, we will dedicate a separate chapter for each one. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage classifies the sects that deny prophethood and critiques their epistemological method. The author begins by stating that reports (khabar) do not produce certain knowledge (al-ilm al-yaqini) but only probability (zann). Since the issue of prophethood is a certain one, basing its proof on a probabilistic method is invalid (bāṭil fāsid). This establishes the flawed foundation of the deniers' arguments. Then the author identifies five sects that attack prophethood by attacking miracles (muʿjizāt). Among them, he elaborates on the fifth sect: they claim that transmitted reports about the prophets contain things unworthy of scholarly attention, thus casting doubt on their prophethood. Then he mentions a sixth sect that argues if God intended to send messengers, they would be angels with overwhelming consecutive miracles; the author notes that this view is even narrated in the Quran. After this enumeration, the author states that the discussion on God being a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) rather than a necessary agent (mūjib bi-dhāt) has been fully covered earlier, so no need to repeat. Finally, he announces that separate chapters will be devoted to the remaining schools. The passage thus serves as a transition, summarizing the sects addressed and outlining the book's structure.

Key Terms
خبر
علم يقيني
ظن
نبوات
معجزات
فرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any report can serve as certain proof for prophethood.
Correction: The author clarifies that reports only yield probability, not certainty, so they cannot be the foundation for proving a certain matter like prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it invalid to prove prophethood using reports (khabar)?
A) Because reports are always false
B) Because reports only yield probability, but prophethood requires certainty
C) Because the Quran forbids using reports
D) Because the five sects reject all reports

Key Takeaway
Reports yield only probability, so they cannot prove a certain matter like prophethood; this invalidates the arguments of sects that attack prophethood via miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend your belief in prophethood, do you rely on arguments that are only probable? How can you ensure your conviction is built on certain proof?
?
Are there aspects of your faith that you hold based on hearsay rather than certain knowledge? What would it take for you to seek certainty in those areas?
?
The author says using probability for a certain matter is corrupt. Are there areas in your life where you are content with probability instead of pursuing certainty? What is at stake if you remain in probability?

The Fifth and Sixth Sects Denying Prophethood
Author describes two additional sects denying prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific arguments of the fifth and sixth sects that deny prophethood, as outlined by the author.

Core Idea
Deniers use transmitted unbecoming reports or insist on angelic messengers to reject prophethood.

After the first five sects that attack prophethood through miracles, the author turns to two additional groups. The fifth sect claims that transmitted reports about the prophets contain unbecoming incidents unworthy of scholarly attention, thus casting doubt on their prophethood. The sixth sect argues that if God intended to send messengers, they would be angels with overwhelming consecutive miracles. Both arguments are rooted in a flawed methodology or expectations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the fifth sect
Read the Arabic and translation for the fifth sect. Note their claim: transmitted reports about prophets contain unbecoming circumstances that discredit their prophethood.
The author does not detail which reports they refer to.
2
Read the passage on the sixth sect
Read the Arabic and translation for the sixth sect. Note their argument: if God sent messengers, they should be angels with overpowering consecutive miracles.
The author notes this view is even quoted in the Quran.
3
Reflect on the author's critique
Consider how the epistemological principle from the previous lesson applies here. Both sects rely on probabilistic reports or arbitrary expectations.
The author does not give a detailed rebuttal here; he will address them in later chapters.

Summary and Structural Outline
Author summarizes enumeration and outlines book structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author summarizes the enumerated sects and outlines the remaining structure of the work.

Core Idea
After enumerating the deniers, the author transitions to prior discussions and future chapters.

Having listed the sects that deny prophethood, the author provides a brief summary and then notes that the discussion on God being a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) versus a necessary agent (mūjib bi-dhāt) has already been covered in full. He announces that separate chapters will be devoted to each of the remaining schools, indicating the book's organizational plan.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read the Arabic and translation. The author signals he has now indicated (ishāra) the enumeration of the deniers.
2
Note the reference to prior discussion
The author refers back to a previous discussion on God being a free agent vs. necessary agent, stating it is not repeated.
This shows the book's cumulative argument.
3
Understand the structural announcement
The author says 'as for the remaining schools, we will dedicate a separate chapter for each one.' This outlines the book's upcoming sections.

From Invalidity of Obligation to Invalidity of Prophethood
Razi argues that invalidity of obligation entails invalidity of prophethood, citing Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi argues that if moral obligation is invalid, then prophethood must also be invalid, and how this argument appears in the Quran.

Core Idea
If prophets only convey obligations, then no obligations means no prophets.

This passage presents the third premise in Razi's argument against determinism. He claims that the very purpose of prophethood is to convey moral obligations. If obligations are invalid, then sending prophets becomes pointless. This argument, rooted in Quranic verses, is a key part of the debate on free will and determinism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from premise to conclusion.
The passage is a continuous argument.
2
Identify the premises
Identify the two key claims: (1) prophets' only purpose is to convey obligations, and (2) obligations are false. See how they lead to the conclusion that prophethood is false.
3
Connect to the Quranic verse
Notice that the Quran cites the polytheists making a similar argument. Ask yourself: Why does Razi include this citation? What does it show?
This connects the argument to a real historical debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثالثة: في بيان أنه لما كان القول ببطلان التكليف حقاً، كان القول ببطلان النبوة حقاً، فتقريره: أن الأنبياء بصريح لسانهم، وفصيح بيانهم، يذكرون أنه لا فائدة في بعثتهم ولا مقصود من إرسالهم إلا تبليغ التكاليف، وأنه لولا هذا المقصود، لم يكن في إرسالهم فائدة البتة. وإذا ثبت هذا، وثبت أن القول بالتكليف باطل، كان القول بإرسالهم باطلاً. وهذه الشبهة في نفي النبوات، قد حكاها الله تعالى في القرآن مراراً، وهو قوله تعالى: (سيقول الذين أشركوا: لو شاء الله ما أشركنا، ولا آباؤنا، ولا حرمنا من شيء كذلك كذب الذين من قبلهم حتى ذاقوا بأسنا) والله أعلم.
As for the third premise: it is an exposition that when the statement of the invalidity of moral obligation is true, the statement of the invalidity of prophethood (nubuwwa) is true. Its explanation: The prophets, by their explicit speech and eloquent expression, mention that there is no benefit in their sending and no purpose for their mission except conveying obligations (takalif). And if not for this purpose, there would be no benefit in their sending at all. When this is established, and it is established that the statement of moral obligation is false, then the statement of sending them becomes false. And this specious argument in negating prophethoods has been related by God Almighty in the Quran repeatedly, and it is His statement: 'Those who associated partners will say: If God had willed, we would not have associated partners, nor our fathers, nor would we have forbidden anything. Thus did those before them deny until they tasted Our might.' And God knows best.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is the third premise in a series arguing against determinism's implications. It claims that if moral obligation (taklif) is invalid, then prophethood (nubuwwa) is also invalid. The reason is that prophets themselves state that the sole purpose of their mission is to convey obligations. If obligations are void, then sending prophets becomes pointless. The author then notes that this very argument—that if God wills, people would not do otherwise—was used by the polytheists to deny prophethood, as cited in the Quran (6:148). Thus, the argument is presented as a false inference from determinism, but it is used here to show the consequences of accepting determinism.

Key Terms
النبوة (al-nubuwwa)
التكاليف (al-takalif)
الشبهة (al-shubha)
نفي النبوات (nafy al-nubuwwat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Razi himself accepts the conclusion that prophethood is invalid.
Correction: Razi is presenting this argument as a reductio ad absurdum of determinism. He does not endorse the conclusion; rather, he uses it to show what follows if determinism is true.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the sole purpose of sending prophets?
A) To perform miracles
B) To convey obligations (takalif)
C) To establish political authority
D) To reveal the unseen

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that if moral obligation is invalid, prophethood becomes purposeless, as prophets exist solely to convey obligations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If prophets were sent only to convey obligations, what does that reveal about the weight of those obligations in your life? Are you treating any divine command as optional advice rather than a binding duty?
?
The polytheists' argument seemed logical on the surface but led to rejecting prophethood. What false beliefs have you entertained that sound reasonable but actually undermine your faith? Commit today to identifying and uprooting one such belief with evidence from revelation.

Four Reasons for the Benefit of Sending Prophets
Lists the objectors' four arguments for the goodness of sending prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four arguments that objectors use to argue that sending prophets is good despite the sufficiency of reason.

Core Idea
Prophets are not redundant; they reinforce, reveal, guide, and aid reason.

Before refuting the claim that reason alone suffices, the author presents the opponent's strongest case. The objectors offer four distinct reasons why sending prophets is beneficial even if reason could theoretically guide us. This lesson walks through each of these arguments, which set the stage for the author's counter-arguments.

Guided Path
1
First Reason: Reinforcement
Read the first reason: prophets reinforce what is already known by reason. Notice that this acknowledges reason's capability but adds that reinforcement is beneficial.
This reason assumes reason can know some truths but needs confirmation.
2
Second Reason: Revealing Hidden Benefits
Read the second reason: some benefits cannot be discovered by reason alone. Prophets reveal these interests, making their mission good.
This reason points to limitations of reason in discovering all goods.
3
Third Reason: Deficient Intellects
Read the third reason: human intellects are deficient in knowing God and the modality of worship. Prophets provide guidance.
This reason focuses on the practical inability of reason to achieve complete knowledge.
4
Fourth Reason: Aids for Rational Obligations
Read the fourth reason: religious obligations serve as aids (alṭāf) to motivate fulfilling rational duties. Note that this is attributed to the Mu'tazila.
This reason introduces a theological concept from the Mu'tazila school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال تحسن بعثة الأنبياء والرسل ؟ لوجوه : الأول : تأكيد ما في العقول الثاني : إنه قد يحصل في بعض الأشياء منافع ومصالح ، لا يمكن الوقوف عليها ، بمجرد العقول، فتحسن بعثة الأنبياء والرسل، ليدلوا عليها ، ويعرفوا الخلق ما فيها من المنافع والمصالح الثالث : إن عقول الخلق ناقصة قاصرة عن معرفة الله تعالى ومعرفة كيفية طاعاته . فكانت الحكمة في بعثة الأنبياء والرسل عليهم السلام : إرشاداً للخلق ، إلى معرفة ذات الله تعالى وصفاته ، ومعرفة كيفية طاعاته . الرابع : إنما أمر الله تعالى بهذه العبادات الشرعية ، لتكون [ الطافاً (۳)] في الواجبات العقلية . وهذا قول المعتزلة .
For they said: Why is it not permissible to say that sending prophets and messengers is good? For reasons: First: to reinforce what is in the intellects. Second: indeed, there may occur in some things benefits and interests that cannot be grasped by mere intellects, so sending prophets and messengers is good so that they point to them and make the creation know the benefits and interests in them. Third: indeed, the intellects of the creation are deficient and incapable of knowing God Most High and knowing the modality of His obedience. So wisdom lay in sending prophets and messengers, peace be upon them, as guidance for the creation to knowledge of the essence of God Most High and His attributes, and knowledge of the modality of His obedience. Fourth: God Most High only commanded these religious acts of worship to be [aids (3)] for the rational obligations. And this is the saying of the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's case against the author's earlier conclusion that reason suffices. The objectors give four reasons why sending prophets is good despite reason's sufficiency: (1) Prophets reinforce what is already known by reason. (2) They reveal benefits and interests that cannot be discovered by reason alone. (3) Human intellects are deficient in knowing God and the manner of worship, so prophets guide them. (4) Religious obligations act as aids (alṭāf) to motivate the performance of rational obligations. The last reason is attributed to the Mu'tazila. This enumeration sets up the author's refutation that follows.

تأكيد
منافع ومصالح
عقول قاصرة
ألطاف
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These four reasons are the author's own view.
Correction: They are presented as objections from opponents that the author will refute.

Checkpoint

A) To introduce new religious laws not known by reason
B) To reinforce what is in the intellects
C) To reveal benefits and interests unknown to reason
D) To serve as aids for rational obligations

Key Takeaway
The objectors argue that prophets are necessary to reinforce reason, reveal hidden benefits, compensate for intellectual deficiency, and aid rational obligations.

Refutation of Reinforcement of Reason
Refutes the claim that prophets reinforce reason

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that prophets reinforce rational knowledge is self-contradictory according to this classical argument.

Core Idea
Reinforcement is unnecessary when the purpose is already achieved, and it can backfire.

Theologians sometimes argue that prophets are needed to reinforce what reason already knows. In this passage, the author refutes this by showing that if reason already knows good and evil, reinforcement is superfluous, and the prophets' own warnings of punishment actually cause harm. This makes the claim self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim being refuted
The claim is that prophets are needed to reinforce rational knowledge. Read the passage to see how the author attacks this idea.
2
Analyze the first argument: redundancy
The author argues that if reason already knows the original purpose, any additional reinforcement is unnecessary because its degrees are not fixed. More reinforcement could always be added, but the core purpose is already achieved.
3
Analyze the second argument: contradiction
The purpose of reinforcement is to prevent harm, but prophets threaten eternal punishment for those who reject them. This causes the greatest harm, contradicting the very purpose of reinforcement.
Notice the logical conclusion: the sending becomes a cause for the worst harm.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Combine the two parts: the claim is weak because it is either unnecessary (if reason is independent) or counterproductive (if it leads to eternal punishment). The author concludes it is contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : أما الأول : فضعيف . لأنه لما كان العقل مستقلاً بمعرفة وجوه الحـ [؟] كان أصل المقصود حاصلاً، وأما مراتب التأكيد فغير مضبوطة. فإنه لو حصل مع ذلك النبي جمع من الملائكة، كان التأكيد أكثر، ولو حصل معه جمع من العساكر المعصومين، لكان التأكيد أبلغ. ولكن لما حصل المقصود الأصلي، لم يكن إلى هذه الزوائد حاجة. فكذا ههنا. ولأن المقصود من التأكيد : السعي في دفع المفسدة، بأقصى الوجوه ، والأنبياء - عليهم السلام ـ يقولون : إن من أعرض عن متابعتنـا فـإنـه يستحق أعظم العقاب . وعلى هذا التقدير تصير البعثة سبباً لأعظم أنواع المفاسد . وهو استحقاق العذاب الدائم، على تقدير مخالفتهم ، وترك متابعتهم . وهذا متناقض .
We say: As for the first [reason]: it is weak. Because when the intellect is independent in knowledge of aspects of the [?], the original purpose is obtained, and the degrees of reinforcement are not fixed. For if along with that prophet there were a host of angels, the reinforcement would be greater, and if there were with him a host of infallible armies, the reinforcement would be more effective. But since the original purpose is obtained, there is no need for these additions. So here likewise. And because the purpose of reinforcement is to strive to repel harm in the utmost ways, and the prophets, peace be upon them, say: Whoever turns away from following us deserves the greatest punishment. On this basis, the sending becomes a cause for the greatest types of harm, namely deserving eternal punishment on the condition of opposing them and abandoning following them. And this is contradictory.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is the author's refutation of the first objection that prophets reinforce rational knowledge. The author argues: (a) If reason already independently knows the aspects of good and evil (the missing word is likely 'al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ'), then the original purpose is already achieved. Reinforcement is not needed because it is indeterminate—more reinforcement could always be added indefinitely. (b) The purpose of reinforcement is to prevent harm, but the prophets themselves threaten eternal punishment for those who reject them, which would cause the greatest harm. Thus, the very claim that sending prophets is beneficial turns out to be self-contradictory, as it leads to the worst possible outcome for those who disobey.

Key Terms
تأكيد
مقصود أصلي
مفسدة
عذاب دائم
متناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets are always beneficial because they reinforce reason.
Correction: The author argues that if reason is already independent, reinforcement is indeterminate and can lead to greater harm, making the sending contradictory.

Checkpoint

C) Reinforcement is unnecessary and leads to eternal punishment for some.
D) Prophets do not threaten punishment.

Key Takeaway
The claim that prophets reinforce reason is self-contradictory because it assumes reason already knows the original purpose, and the prophets' threats of punishment cause the very harm they are supposed to prevent.

The Contradiction in Prophetic Threats
Argues that prophetic threats make the mission cause the greatest harm, creating a contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradiction that arises when prophetic threats of punishment are considered as part of the mission's purpose to prevent harm.

Core Idea
Prophethood's mission to prevent harm becomes the cause of the greatest harm.

This passage presents a powerful objection against the necessity of prophethood. It argues that if the purpose of prophetic emphasis is to prevent harm, then threatening the worst punishment for rejection actually makes the mission the source of the greatest harm. This creates a contradiction that must be resolved. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general principle
Read the opening sentence about good, evil, benefit, harm, and the idea that the original purpose is already attained while levels of emphasis are not fixed.
Notice how the author first establishes a general rule before applying it to the prophetic case.
2
Consider the analogy
Analyze the examples of angels and infallible troops to see how extra emphasis is unnecessary once the core purpose is achieved.
This analogy sets up the expectation that prophets should not need extreme threats if the mission already accomplishes its goal.
3
Examine the purpose of emphasis
The passage states that the purpose of emphasis is to repel harm in the most extreme ways. Keep this definition in mind.
4
Apply to prophetic threats
Prophets threaten the greatest punishment for those who reject them. This makes the mission itself cause the greatest harm: eternal damnation.
Ask yourself: If the mission is supposed to prevent harm, how can it cause such a severe harm?
5
Recognize the contradiction
The conclusion is that the mission becomes the source of the greatest harm, contradicting its purpose. Reflect on why this is a strong objection.
This is a logical critique, not a denial of prophethood itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقبح، والمصلحة والمفسدة، كان أصل المقصود حاصلاً، وأما مراتب التأكيد فغير مضبوطة. فإنه لو حصل مع ذلك النبي جمع من الملائكة، كان التأكيد أكثر، ولو حصل معه جمع من العساكر المعصومين، لكان التأكيد أبلغ. ولكن لما حصل المقصود الأصلي، لم يكن إلى هذه الزوائد حاجة. فكذا ههنا. ولأن المقصود من التأكيد: السعي في دفع المفسدة، بأقصى الوجوه، والأنبياء عليهم السلام يقولون: إن من أعرض عن متابعتنا فإنه يستحق أعظم العقاب. وعلى هذا التقدير تصير البعثة سبباً لأعظم أنواع المفاسد. وهو استحقاق العذاب الدائم، على تقدير مخالفتهم، وترك متابعتهم. وهذا متناقض.
And the good and evil, and benefit and harm: the original purpose was attained. As for levels of emphasis, they are not fixed. For if along with that the prophet had a company of angels, the emphasis would be more, and if with him there was a company of infallible troops, the emphasis would be stronger. But when the original purpose is attained, there is no need for these extras. Likewise here. And because the purpose of emphasis is to strive to repel harm in the most extreme ways, and the prophets, peace be upon them, say: 'Whoever turns away from following us deserves the greatest punishment.' On this assumption, the mission itself becomes the cause of the greatest types of harm—namely, deserving eternal punishment on the assumption of their opposition and abandoning their following. And this is contradictory.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the necessity of prophethood by highlighting a contradiction in the logic of prophetic mission. The author begins by discussing the concepts of good and evil, benefit and harm, stating that the primary purpose (of emphasis) is already achieved. Levels of additional emphasis (like having angels or infallible armies) are not fixed; once the core purpose is obtained, extras are unnecessary. The key argument then proceeds: the purpose of emphasis (ta'kid) is to ward off harm in the most extreme manner. Yet prophets threaten that those who reject them deserve the greatest punishment. On this basis, the mission itself (ba'tha) gives rise to the greatest harm—eternal damnation for those who oppose. Since the prophets claim their mission prevents harm, but in fact it causes the ultimate harm, this is a contradiction. The objection is that prophethood cannot be necessary if it inherently leads to such severe negative consequences for those who disbelieve.

المصلحة
المفسدة
التأكيد
البعثة
متناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage denies prophethood or suggests prophets are harmful.
Correction: This is not a denial of prophethood but a logical critique of a specific justification that prophethood is meant to prevent harm.

Checkpoint

B) If emphasis is to prevent harm, then prophets' threats of punishment make the mission the cause of greatest harm.
C) The mission of prophets requires too many angels to be practical.
D) The purpose of emphasis is unclear.

Key Takeaway
The objection shows that if prophethood is meant to prevent harm, then its own threats of severe punishment create the greatest harm, leading to a contradiction.

Prophetic Benefits: Necessary or Not?
Refutes the claim that prophetic benefits necessitate prophethood by dividing benefits into necessary/non-necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that prophethood alerts to necessary benefits is refuted by showing that necessary benefits are already known, and non-necessary benefits cause no harm.

Core Idea
Prophetic benefits are either already known or irrelevant, so they cannot justify prophethood.

The author continues his refutation of arguments for the necessity of prophethood. Here he tackles a second claim: that the purpose of the prophetic mission is to draw attention to the benefits inherent in things. He responds with a logical distinction: if the benefits are necessary, they are already known to everyone; if they are not necessary, their absence causes no harm. Thus, prophethood is not needed to teach these benefits.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the two conditional branches the author offers.
The passage uses a logical 'if... then' structure.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The author divides the benefits into two categories: necessary for life and non-necessary. Understand the implication of each.
Necessary benefits are those without which life cannot continue.
3
Understand the logical force
If benefits are necessary, they are already known by everyone, so prophethood is redundant. If not necessary, their loss causes no harm, so no need for a prophet to warn about them.
This refutes the claim that prophethood is needed to alert to benefits.
4
Connect to the overall refutation
Consider how this argument fits into the broader discussion of prophethood's necessity. The author is systematically dismantling reasons given by opponents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني: وهو قوله: (المقصود من البعثة: التنبيه على ما في الأشياء من المنافع)، فنقول: تلك المنافع إن كانت ضرورية التحصيل، كانت معلومة للكل. لأن على هذا التقدير لا تحصل الحياة إلا بها. وإن كانت غير ضرورية التحصيل، فحينئذ لا يلزم من فواتها حصول ضرر أصلاً، فوجب الاحتراز عنها. على ما بيناه في تقرير الدليل. وأما قوله: وهذه العبادات ألطاف في فعل الواجبات العقلية، فنقول: هذا باطل لوجهين: الأول: إن معنى كون فعل الأول لطفاً في فعل الثاني: هو أن فعل الأول يدعو الفاعل إلى فعل الثاني. وهذا المعنى بتقدير حصوله، يكون حالة وجدانية من النفس بالضرورة. ونحن البتة لا نجد من أنفسنا: الإتيان بالصلاة والصوم يدعونا إلى رد الوديعة ويحملنا على ترك الظلم. فثبت: أن هذه الحالة لو كانت موجودة، لكان العلم بحصولها: علماً ضرورياً. وثبت: أن هذا العلم الضروري مفقود، فوجب أن لا يكون هذا المعنى حاصلاً. والثاني: إنه لو كان وجوب هذه العبادات الشرعية، لأجل أن تصير ألطافاً في الواجبات العقلية، لكان المكلف إذا رد الوديعة، وترك الظلم؛ وجب أن لا يجب عليه شيء من هذه العبادات. لأن بعد حصول المطلوب فيه، كان إيجاب اللطف عبثاً. وذلك عندكم باطل.
And as for the second aspect—that is, his statement: 'The purpose of the mission is to draw attention to the benefits in things'—we say: Those benefits, if they are necessary to obtain, are known to all. Because on this assumption, life is not attained except through them. And if they are not necessary to obtain, then their loss does not entail any harm at all; so it is necessary to guard against them. As we have explained in the exposition of the proof. And as for his statement: 'These acts of worship are graces (alṭāf) in performing the rational duties,' we say: This is false for two reasons. The first: The meaning of an act being a grace (luṭf) in another act is that the first act calls the agent to the second act. This meaning, if it obtains, is necessarily a psychological state of the soul. And we absolutely do not find in ourselves that performing prayer and fasting calls us to return a deposit and compels us to abandon injustice. So it is established that if this state existed, knowledge of its existence would be necessary knowledge. And it is established that this necessary knowledge is absent, so necessarily this meaning does not obtain. The second: If the obligation of these legal acts of worship were so that they become graces in the rational duties, then if the morally responsible person returns the deposit and abandons injustice, it would be necessary that none of these acts of worship be obligatory upon him. Because after the desired end is attained in that, making the grace obligatory would be futile. And that is false according to you.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of arguments for the necessity of prophethood. The author addresses a second claim: that the purpose of the prophetic mission is to alert people to the benefits inherent in things. The response is twofold: if these benefits are necessary for life, they are already known to everyone (hence prophethood is redundant); if they are not necessary, their absence causes no harm, so no need for a prophet to warn about them. The author then tackles another claim: that the prescribed acts of worship serve as 'graces' (alṭāf) that facilitate the performance of rational duties (like justice and honesty). This is refuted with two arguments. First, if an act of worship were a grace for a rational duty, it would produce a psychological state that impels the agent toward the duty. But we do not find that prayer or fasting compels us to return deposits or abandon injustice. If such a state existed, it would be known necessarily, but it is not; therefore the claimed connection is false. Second, if the legal obligations were only meant to be graces, then once the rational duty is fulfilled (e.g., returning a deposit), the worship would no longer be obligatory—which is absurd and contrary to the actual requirement of continuous worship. Thus the claim that prophethood is needed to provide these graces is invalid.

Key Terms
الوجه الثاني
المنافع
ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think prophethood is necessary because it reveals unknown benefits necessary for life.
Correction: Actually, any benefit truly necessary for life is already known by everyone, so prophethood is redundant. Non-necessary benefits do not require prophetic warning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if the benefits that prophethood is supposed to alert to are necessary for life?
A) They are known only through prophecy.
B) They are already known to everyone.
C) They cause harm if not known.
D) They are unknown to most people.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of prophethood cannot be argued from the benefits of its teachings, because any truly necessary benefit is already known, and non-necessary benefits do not warrant prophetic intervention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a non-necessary benefit you often pursue (e.g., social status, material comfort). How does this lesson reveal that its absence causes no real harm? What would it take for you to reorder your life around only what is truly necessary?
?
If the necessary benefits of faith are already known to you, why do you still neglect them? Is it because you don't truly believe they are necessary for your spiritual life? What one step can you take today to treat them as non-negotiable?
?
When you seek knowledge, do you insist on learning things that are merely interesting (non-necessary) while ignoring foundational truths? How can you prioritize learning the necessary truths that your salvation depends on?

Worship as Grace: Two Refutations
Refutes the claim that worship is a grace (lutf) for rational duties with two logical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments against the claim that worship functions as a grace (lutf) that facilitates rational duties like justice and honesty.

Core Idea
Worship as a grace fails: it neither compels rational duties nor remains necessary once the duty is done.

The author now addresses a different claim: that the prescribed acts of worship are 'graces' (alṭāf) that aid in performing rational duties like returning deposits and avoiding injustice. He provides two clear arguments showing why this claim is false. The first argument is based on internal experience: we do not find that worship creates a psychological drive to fulfill rational duties. The second is a logical reductio: if worship were only a means to rational duties, it would become optional once those duties are achieved, which is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two arguments
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Look for the two reasons the author gives, marked by 'the first' and 'the second'.
The first argument is about psychological state; the second about obligation.
2
Understand the first argument: psychological state
If worship were a grace, it would produce a psychological state that compels rational duties. But we do not find that prayer or fasting makes us automatically return deposits or avoid injustice.
If such a state existed, it would be known necessarily; since it is not, the claim is false.
3
Understand the second argument: conditional obligation
If worship were only a grace for rational duties, then once a person fulfills those duties (e.g., returns a deposit), worship would no longer be needed. But worship is still required, so the claim is absurd.
This shows that worship has its own independent obligation.
4
Reflect on the combined refutation
Together, these arguments show that the 'grace' claim is not only unsupported by experience but also leads to a logical contradiction with the actual practice of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني: وهو قوله: (المقصود من البعثة: التنبيه على ما في الأشياء من المنافع)، فنقول: تلك المنافع إن كانت ضرورية التحصيل، كانت معلومة للكل. لأن على هذا التقدير لا تحصل الحياة إلا بها. وإن كانت غير ضرورية التحصيل، فحينئذ لا يلزم من فواتها حصول ضرر أصلاً، فوجب الاحتراز عنها. على ما بيناه في تقرير الدليل. وأما قوله: وهذه العبادات ألطاف في فعل الواجبات العقلية، فنقول: هذا باطل لوجهين: الأول: إن معنى كون فعل الأول لطفاً في فعل الثاني: هو أن فعل الأول يدعو الفاعل إلى فعل الثاني. وهذا المعنى بتقدير حصوله، يكون حالة وجدانية من النفس بالضرورة. ونحن البتة لا نجد من أنفسنا: الإتيان بالصلاة والصوم يدعونا إلى رد الوديعة ويحملنا على ترك الظلم. فثبت: أن هذه الحالة لو كانت موجودة، لكان العلم بحصولها: علماً ضرورياً. وثبت: أن هذا العلم الضروري مفقود، فوجب أن لا يكون هذا المعنى حاصلاً. والثاني: إنه لو كان وجوب هذه العبادات الشرعية، لأجل أن تصير ألطافاً في الواجبات العقلية، لكان المكلف إذا رد الوديعة، وترك الظلم؛ وجب أن لا يجب عليه شيء من هذه العبادات. لأن بعد حصول المطلوب فيه، كان إيجاب اللطف عبثاً. وذلك عندكم باطل.
And as for the second aspect—that is, his statement: 'The purpose of the mission is to draw attention to the benefits in things'—we say: Those benefits, if they are necessary to obtain, are known to all. Because on this assumption, life is not attained except through them. And if they are not necessary to obtain, then their loss does not entail any harm at all; so it is necessary to guard against them. As we have explained in the exposition of the proof. And as for his statement: 'These acts of worship are graces (alṭāf) in performing the rational duties,' we say: This is false for two reasons. The first: The meaning of an act being a grace (luṭf) in another act is that the first act calls the agent to the second act. This meaning, if it obtains, is necessarily a psychological state of the soul. And we absolutely do not find in ourselves that performing prayer and fasting calls us to return a deposit and compels us to abandon injustice. So it is established that if this state existed, knowledge of its existence would be necessary knowledge. And it is established that this necessary knowledge is absent, so necessarily this meaning does not obtain. The second: If the obligation of these legal acts of worship were so that they become graces in the rational duties, then if the morally responsible person returns the deposit and abandons injustice, it would be necessary that none of these acts of worship be obligatory upon him. Because after the desired end is attained in that, making the grace obligatory would be futile. And that is false according to you.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of arguments for the necessity of prophethood. The author addresses a second claim: that the purpose of the prophetic mission is to alert people to the benefits inherent in things. The response is twofold: if these benefits are necessary for life, they are already known to everyone (hence prophethood is redundant); if they are not necessary, their absence causes no harm, so no need for a prophet to warn about them. The author then tackles another claim: that the prescribed acts of worship serve as 'graces' (alṭāf) that facilitate the performance of rational duties (like justice and honesty). This is refuted with two arguments. First, if an act of worship were a grace for a rational duty, it would produce a psychological state that impels the agent toward the duty. But we do not find that prayer or fasting compels us to return deposits or abandon injustice. If such a state existed, it would be known necessarily, but it is not; therefore the claimed connection is false. Second, if the legal obligations were only meant to be graces, then once the rational duty is fulfilled (e.g., returning a deposit), the worship would no longer be obligatory—which is absurd and contrary to the actual requirement of continuous worship. Thus the claim that prophethood is needed to provide these graces is invalid.

Key Terms
لطف
الواجبات العقلية
حالة وجدانية
موجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that worship is merely a tool to help us do good deeds and rational duties, and without worship we cannot be just.
Correction: Worship is not a psychological coercive force; its obligation is independent of whether it leads to rational duties. People can and do fulfill rational duties without worship, and worship is required even after moral acts are performed.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why is worship not a grace (lutf)?
A) Because worship is only for otherworldly rewards.
B) Because we do not experience a psychological state from worship that compels us to do rational duties.
C) Because rational duties are impossible without worship.
D) Because worship is forbidden when doing rational duties.

Key Takeaway
Worship's purpose is not to be a grace for rational duties; its obligation stands independently, and it does not produce the psychological coercion that such a grace would require.

?
The author says we do not find that prayer and fasting compel us to abandon injustice. Have you ever relied on your worship as a shield against sin, only to feel it didn't work? Does that mean worship is useless? What does this lesson say about that?
