First Doubt: Actions and Motives Undermine Miracles as Proof
The author introduces the first doubt with two divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first doubt against the evidential value of miracles, which argues that whether actions depend on motives or not, the link between miracles and truth is not logically necessary.

Core Idea
This doubt challenges whether miracles can be certain proof of prophethood.

After establishing that God is the Creator of miracles, skeptics raise a doubt: does that automatically prove the prophet's truthfulness? They argue that the logical connection is not straightforward. This doubt revolves around whether human actions require motives, and whether God is the ultimate cause of all events, including evil deeds. Understanding this doubt is crucial to appreciate the refutation that follows in the subsequent lessons.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the chapter title sets up the doubt: even if God creates miracles, that does not automatically confirm the claimant.
The passage begins with the chapter heading, which is part of the source.
2
Identify the two divisions of the doubt
The doubt presents two possibilities: (1) actions depend on motives, or (2) actions do not depend on motives. Follow how each leads to a different problem for miracles as proof.
The first division leads to God being agent of all actions; the second leads to uncertainty about God's purpose.
3
Analyze the first division
If actions depend on motives, then human actions are caused by motives that ultimately come from God. Thus God is the agent of all actions, including evil deeds. Consequently, God could also show a miracle on the hand of a liar, so miracles no longer prove truth.
Notice the principle: 'the agent of the cause is the agent of the effect'.
4
Analyze the second division
If actions do not depend on motives, then God could create a miracle without any motive or purpose. This possibility prevents us from being certain that the miracle was created specifically to confirm the claimant.
The second division also breaks the logical certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع في حكاية شبهات القائلين بأن على تقدير أن يثبت أن خالق المعجزات هو الله سبحانه وتعالى الا أن ذلك لا يدل على أنه تعالى انما خلقها لأجل تصديق المدعي للرسالة
فالشبهة الأولى : أن يقال : الفعل إما أن يتوقف على الدواعي أو لا يتوقف . فإن توقف الفعل على الدواعي ، فصدور الفعل منا يتوقف على حصول الدواعي لنا . وحدوث تلك الدواعي يكون من الأسباب العالية ، وحينئذ تكون جميع القبائح الصادرة من العباد ، معلولة وموجبة عن فعل الله تعالى ، وفاعل السبب فاعل للمسبب ، فحينئذ يكون فاعل جميع القبائح هو تعالى وتقدس . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فحينئذ لا يمتنع من الله تعالى إظهار المعجز على يد الكاذب . وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه لا يبقى المعجز دليلاً على الصدق على القسم الأول
وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على انضمام الدواعي إليه . فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير لا يمتنع أن يقال : إنه تعالى خلق هذا المعجز لا لشيء من الدواعي والأغراض أصلا . وإذا كان هذا الاحتمال ، امتنع القطع بأنه تعالى ما خلق هذا المعجز لغرض تصديق المدعي
Chapter Seven: Narrating the doubts of those who say that even if it is established that the Creator of miracles is Allah, that does not indicate that He only created them for the purpose of confirming the claimant to prophethood.
The first doubt: that it is said: action either depends on motives or does not depend. If action depends on motives, then the occurrence of action from us depends on the occurrence of motives for us. And the occurrence of those motives is from the higher causes, and then all evil deeds issuing from servants are effects and necessitated by the act of Allah, and the agent of the cause is the agent of the effect, so then the agent of all evil deeds is He, exalted and sanctified. And if this is established, then it is not impossible for Allah to show a miracle on the hand of the liar. And on this supposition, the miracle does not remain a proof of truthfulness according to the first division.
As for the second division, which is that the occurrence of an act from the capable does not depend on the addition of motives to him, we say: on this supposition, it is not impossible to say that He, exalted, created this miracle for nothing of motives and purposes at all. And if this possibility exists, then certainty is precluded that He, exalted, did not create this miracle for the purpose of confirming the claimant.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new chapter that presents doubts against the evidential value of miracles even if God is the Creator. The author titles the chapter 'Narrating the doubts of those who say that even if it is established that the Creator of miracles is Allah, that does not indicate that He only created them to confirm the claimant to prophethood.' The first doubt (al-shubhah al-ula) is then presented. It sets up a dichotomy: actions either depend on motives (dawa'i) or do not. If they depend, then human actions depend on motives that come from higher causes (al-asbab al-'aliya). Consequently, all evil deeds (qaba'ih) from servants are effects of God's act, and the agent of the cause is the agent of the effect, so God becomes the agent of all evil. If that is established, it is not impossible for God to show a miracle on the hand of a liar, so miracles no longer prove truth. If actions do not depend on motives (the second division), then God could create a miracle without any motive or purpose, so we cannot be certain it was for confirmation. Thus, both possibilities undercut the certainty that miracles are proof of prophethood.

Key Terms
شبهة
دواعي
قبائح
فاعل السبب فاعل المسبب
كاذب
قطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God is the Creator of miracles, they automatically prove the truthfulness of the claimant.
Correction: This doubt shows that even if God creates miracles, logical problems remain: either God becomes the agent of all actions (including evil) or the purpose of the miracle becomes uncertain, so the link between miracle and truth is not automatic.

Checkpoint
According to the first doubt, if actions depend on motives, what follows regarding evil deeds?
A) They are caused by human free will.
B) They are effects of God's act.
C) They are impossible.
D) They are neutral.

Key Takeaway
The first doubt challenges the conclusiveness of miracles as proof of prophethood by showing that whether actions depend on motives or not, the link between miracle and truth is not logically necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

First Objection: Action and Motives
First objection against miracles as proof of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against miracles as proof of prophethood: the dilemma of action depending or not depending on motives, and why it challenges the certainty of miracles as divine confirmation.

Core Idea
A dilemma: whether actions depend on motives or not, miracles lose their proof value.

This lesson introduces the first objection raised against the argument that miracles prove prophethood. The objector presents a logical dilemma: either human actions depend on motives or they do not. In both cases, the objector argues, the connection between a miracle and divine confirmation of truthfulness breaks down. Understanding this objection is crucial for appreciating later rebuttals and the depth of theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a dilemma is presented with two horns, each leading to a conclusion that undermines the proof value of miracles.
The objector uses logical necessity to force the conclusion.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Find the two cases: (1) action depends on motives, (2) action does not depend on motives. Notice how each is argued and what conclusion follows.
3
Trace the implications of each horn
For the first horn: if actions depend on motives, all actions are ultimately caused by God, making Him the agent of evil and miracles for a liar possible. For the second: if actions do not depend on motives, God could create miracles without purpose, so we cannot be certain a miracle confirms truth.
This challenges the necessary connection between miracle and truthfulness.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why this objection is serious: it attacks both divine justice and the reliability of miracles as signs of prophecy.
The objection assumes a deterministic causality that some theologians reject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الأولى : أن يقال : الفعل إما أن يتوقف على الدواعي أو لا يتوقف . فإن توقف الفعل على الدواعي ، فصدور الفعل منا يتوقف على حصول الدواعي لنا . وحدوث تلك الدواعي يكون من الأسباب العالية ، وحينئذ تكون جميع القبائح الصادرة من العباد ، معلولة وموجبة عن فعل الله تعالى ، وفاعل السبب فاعل للمسبب ، فحينئذ يكون فاعل جميع القبائح هو تعالى وتقدس . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فحينئذ لا يمتنع من الله تعالى إظهار المعجز على يد الكاذب . وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه لا يبقى المعجز دليلاً على الصدق على القسم الأول . وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على انضمام الدواعي إليه . فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير لا يمتنع أن يقال : إنه تعالى خلق هذا المعجز لا لشيء من الدواعي والأغراض أصلا . وإذا كان هذا الاحتمال ، امتنع القطع بأنه تعالى ما خلق هذا المعجز لغرض تصديق المدعي .
The first objection: It is said: The action either depends on motives or does not depend. If the action depends on motives, then the occurrence of action from us depends on the occurrence of motives for us. And the occurrence of those motives is from the higher causes, and then all evil deeds emanating from servants are caused and necessitated by the act of God the Exalted, and the agent of the cause is the agent of the effect, so then the agent of all evil deeds is He, Exalted and Holy. And if this is established, then it is not impossible for God the Exalted to manifest a miracle at the hand of a liar. And on this assumption, the miracle does not remain a proof of truthfulness in the first case. As for the second case, which is that the occurrence of an action from the capable does not depend on the addition of motives to it, we say: on this assumption, it is not impossible to say that He the Exalted created this miracle not for any motives or purposes at all. And if this possibility exists, it is impossible to be certain that God the Exalted did not create this miracle for the purpose of affirming the claimant.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is an objection against the argument that miracles indicate the truthfulness of a prophet. The objector presents a dilemma: either actions depend on motives or they do not. In the first case, if actions depend on motives, then all human actions are ultimately caused by God through a chain of causes, making God the agent of all evil. Consequently, God could also create miracles for a liar. In the second case, if actions do not depend on motives, then God could create miracles without any purpose, so we cannot be certain that a miracle was created to affirm a prophet. Thus, in both scenarios, the miracle ceases to be a definitive proof of truthfulness. The objection aims to break the necessary connection between miracles and divine confirmation of prophethood.

Key Terms
دواعي (motives)
أسباب عالية (higher causes)
فاعل السبب فاعل المسبب (agent of cause is agent of effect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection is a definitive refutation of miracles as proof of prophethood.
Correction: The objection is a challenge that will be answered later in the text. It does not stand unanswered; it is part of a dialectical argument.

Checkpoint
In the first objection, what is the conclusion if actions depend on motives?
A) Miracles are always signs of truthfulness.
B) God cannot create miracles at all.
C) It is possible for God to create a miracle for a liar.
D) Human actions are free from divine causality.

Key Takeaway
The first objection uses a dilemma to argue that whether actions depend on motives or not, miracles cannot be certain proofs of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The objector argues that if God causes all actions, then He is the agent of evil deeds. Do you truly believe that God is not responsible for your sins, or do you sometimes blame Him in your heart? Today, will you take full responsibility for a specific wrong you committed, without attributing it to divine decree as an excuse?

Second Objection: God's Actions Have No Purposes
Second objection: God's actions are not caused by purposes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against miracle-as-purpose, which claims that God's actions are not caused by purposes.

Core Idea
God's actions are not for purposes, so miracles cannot serve to affirm a prophet.

This lesson presents the second objection raised against the view that miracles are created to affirm prophethood. The objection rests on a theological premise: that God's actions and judgments are not motivated by purposes or aims. Therefore, using miracles as evidence for prophethood is invalid if they are not purpose-driven.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the key premise: God's actions are not caused by purposes.
2
Understand the implication
Recognize why this premise leads to the conclusion that miracles cannot be for affirming a claimant.
3
Reflect on the challenge
Consider how this objection challenges the teleological argument for prophethood through miracles.
This objection is part of a larger debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : إنا قد ذكرنا الدلائل الكثيرة على أنه يمتنع أن تكون أفعال الله تعالى وأحكامه ، معللة بالأغراض والمقاصد ، وعلى هذا فيمتنع أن يقال : إنه تعالى إنما خلق المعجز لأجل تصديق المدعي .
The second objection: We have mentioned many proofs that it is impossible for the actions of God the Exalted and His decrees to be caused by purposes and aims. And on this, it is impossible to say that He the Exalted only created the miracle for the sake of affirming the claimant.

This passage is the second objection. It asserts that it is established and proven that God's actions and judgments are not motivated by purposes or aims. Therefore, it cannot be claimed that God created a miracle specifically to affirm a claimant's prophethood. This objection relies on a theological position that God does not act for reasons or objectives, which would nullify any teleological explanation of miracles.

The Third Objection: Purposeless Divine Actions
Third objection: God's actions may lack purpose; example of world's creation timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the argument from miracles, which claims that God can act without purpose, and the beginning of the response that the specification of the world's creation time cannot be intrinsic to that time.

Core Idea
If God can create the world at any time without reason, then miracles too could be meaningless.

The third objection challenges the link between miracles and divine confirmation. It argues that God's actions are not always purposeful, using the very foundation of the kalam argument – the temporal origination of the world – as evidence. The objector claims that the world's creation at a specific time without a specifier shows that purposeless acts are possible, so miracles could also be purposeless. This lesson presents this objection and the initial refutation that the time itself cannot be the cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objector concedes that some actions have purposes but asserts that others do not, using the creation timing as a key example.
2
Examine the example
Focus on the claim that the world's creation at a specific time without a specifier (مخصص or مرجح) shows God can act without purpose. This is used to argue that miracles might also be purposeless.
The objector builds on the kalam framework itself.
3
Analyze the sub-argument
The objector then argues that the specification of the time cannot be due to the time itself, because that would make time the cause of the world, undermining the proof of a Creator. See how this reasoning is meant to block a possible response.
The passage ends before completing the response, but the direction is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : هب أن أفعال الله تعالى وأحكامه قد تكون محللة بالدواعي والأغراض إلا أنه قد تكون أيضاً غير معللة بالدواعي والأغراض والدليل عليه : أن القول بإثبات النبوة ، فرع على إثبات حدوث العالم وهذه القاعدة لا يمكن تقريرها ، إلا إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى خصص إحداث العالم بوقت معين ، لا لمخصص ولا لمرجح البتة . وهذا يقتضي القطع بأن الله تعالى قد يفعل الفعل ، لا لغرض ولا لداع أصلاً . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون خلق المعجزات من هذا الباب ؟ وعلى هذا التقدير ، لا يدل المعجز على التصديق ، فنفتقر ههنا إلى بيان أن تخصيص إحداث العالم بالوقت المعين ، لا يمكن أن يكون معللاً بشيء من الأغراض والدواعي . والدليل عليه : أن اختصاص ذلك الوقت بذلك الغرض المعين. إما أن يكون لذاته أو لغيره . ولا جائز أن يكون لذاته . لأنه لو جاز أن يختص ذلك الوقت بذلك الغرض المعين لذاته ، فحينئذ لم يبعد أن يختص ذلك الوقت بسائر الآثار المخصوصة به وإذا جاز ذلك لم يمتنع أن يقال : المقتضي لحدوث العالم في ذلك الوقت هو نفس ذلك الوقت . وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه لا يمكن الاستدلال بحدوث العالم على وجود الصانع . وأما إن كان اختصاص ذلك الوقت بتلك الخاصية ليس لذاته بل لأجل أن فاعل العالم ، خصص ذلك الوقت بذلك الغرض ، وبتلك الخاصية
The third objection: Suppose that the actions of God the Exalted and His decrees may be caused by motives and purposes, but they may also be not caused by motives and purposes. The proof of that is: The statement of establishing prophethood is a branch of establishing the temporal origination of the world, and this principle cannot be established unless we say: He the Exalted specified the creation of the world at a particular time, without any specifier or preponderant at all. And this necessitates the certainty that God the Exalted may do an act with no purpose and no motive at all. And if this is established, then why is it not permissible that the creation of miracles be of this kind? And on this assumption, the miracle does not indicate affirmation, so we need here to clarify that the specification of the creation of the world at the particular time cannot be caused by any of the purposes and motives. The proof of that: That the specification of that time with that particular purpose is either due to itself or due to something else. And it is not permissible that it be due to itself. Because if it were permissible for that time to be specified with that particular purpose due to itself, then it would not be unlikely that that time would be specified with its other specific effects, and if that is permissible, then it is not impossible to say: the prerequisite for the occurrence of the world at that time is that time itself. And on this assumption, it is not possible to infer the existence of the Maker from the temporal origination of the world. But if the specification of that time with that property is not due to itself but because the Maker of the world specified that time with that purpose and that property...
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection. It concedes that some divine actions may have purposes, but argues that others are clearly without purpose, as exemplified by the creation of the world at a specific time with no specifying factor. This shows that God can act without purpose, so miracles could also be purposeless and thus not indicators of truthfulness. The objection then attempts to prove that the specification of the time cannot be due to an intrinsic property of that time, as that would make time itself the cause of the world and undermine the proof of a Creator. The argument cuts off but the thrust is to show that if the time is not intrinsically special, then God's choice is arbitrary and without purpose, supporting the possibility that miracles are also arbitrary.

Key Terms
تخصيص (specification)
مخصص (specifier)
مرجح (preponderant)
حدوث العالم (temporal origination of the world)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example does the third objection use to show that God can act without purpose?
A) The creation of angels
B) The specification of the world's creation at a particular time
C) The sending of prophets
D) The revelation of scriptures

Key Takeaway
The third objection challenges the necessity of purpose in divine actions, using the creation's timing as a counterexample to undermine the evidential value of miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector argues that since God created the world at a specific time with no apparent reason, miracles could also be meaningless. Have you ever felt that a trial in your life came at a 'random' time, leading you to doubt God's care? How does the argument in this passage expose the flaw in that feeling? What can you do this week to actively trust His timing?
?
The passage shows that if time itself had the property of being the right time for creation, it would undermine the proof of God's existence. In your faith, do you ever rely on natural explanations (like 'it was just the right time') to the point of ignoring that God is the One who specifies all times? What concrete step will you take to remind yourself of His sovereignty over every moment?
?
The objector's reasoning leads to a contradiction: either time causes existence or God acts with purpose. How does this challenge your own tendency to assume God's actions are arbitrary when you don't understand them? Will you commit to seeking His wisdom in events you currently find purposeless, rather than dismissing them?

The Second Objection: God's Actions Cannot Be for Purposes
The author presents the second objection: God's actions cannot be for purposes, so miracles cannot authenticate prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the proof of prophethood through miracles, which argues that God's actions cannot be motivated by purposes.

Core Idea
If God's acts have no purposes, miracles cannot authenticate prophets.

This lesson introduces the second objection faced by the proof of prophethood through miracles. The objection asserts that since God's actions are not caused by purposes, a miracle cannot be created for the specific purpose of authenticating a prophet. Understanding this objection is essential for grasping the full dialectic in the discussion of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it begins with 'الشبهة الثانية' (the second objection) and states a key premise.
2
Analyze the premise
Identify the premise: it is impossible for God's actions to be motivated by purposes (أغراض ومقاصد). Reflect on why this might be argued (e.g., God's self-sufficiency).
3
Connect to the conclusion
See how the premise leads to the conclusion: it cannot be said that the miracle was created for the purpose of authenticating the claimant. This undermines the proof of prophethood through miracles.
The objection assumes that a purposeless act cannot serve a specific purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : إنا قد ذكرنا الدلائل الكثيرة على أنه يمتنع أن تكون أفعال الله تعالى وأحكامه ، معللة بالأغراض والمقاصد ، وعلى هذا فيمتنع أن يقال : إنه تعالى إنما خلق المعجز لأجل تصديق المدعي.
The second objection: We have indeed mentioned numerous proofs that it is impossible for the acts of God Most High and His judgments to be motivated by purposes and aims. Upon this, it is impossible to say that He Most High only created the miracle for the sake of authenticating the claimant.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection (الشبهة الثانية) against the proof of prophethood through miracles. The objection states that it has been established by many proofs that God's actions and rulings cannot be caused by purposes (أغراض) and objectives (مقاصد). Therefore, it cannot be said that God created the miracle specifically to authenticate the claimant (تصديق المدعي). The underlying logic is: if God's acts have no purposes, then a miracle cannot have the purpose of authentication, so a miracle cannot serve as evidence for prophethood.

The Third Objection: Actions Without Purpose and the Argument from the Origination of the World
The third objection argues that if some of God's acts are purposeless, then miracles may also be purposeless, undermining the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third objection challenges the link between miracles and authentication by arguing that the proof of the world's origination requires God to act without purpose.

Core Idea
If God can act without purpose, miracles may be purposeless too—breaking the proof of prophethood.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection that goes straight to the heart of the kalam argument for prophethood. The objector grants that some acts may have purpose, but shows that the foundational proof of the world's origination forces us to admit purposeless divine actions. This opens the door to questioning whether miracles themselves are purposeful indicators of truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the objection begins with 'هب' (suppose) and then builds a dilemma.
2
Identify the objection's main premise
The objector grants that some of God's acts may have purpose but argues that it is also possible that some do not. Find the proof he offers for this possibility from the proof of the world's origination.
The key phrase: 'لا لمخصص ولا لمرجح البتة' (with no particularizer and no preference at all).
3
Examine the dilemma about the time of creation
The objector argues that the specification of the time of creation cannot be for a purpose. He presents two possibilities: either the time's special property inheres in its essence or comes from an external cause. See how he refutes both.
The first horn leads to the time being the cause of the world; the second leads to infinite regress.
4
Grasp the conclusion
After refuting both possibilities, the objector concludes that the only consistent way to prove the world's origination is to say that the free agent acts without purpose. Therefore, we cannot be certain that miracles are created specifically for authentication.
This conclusion directly undermines the evidential value of miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : هب أن أفعال الله تعالى وأحكامه قد تكون معللة بالدواعي والأغراض إلا أنه قد تكون أيضاً غير معللة بالدواعي والأغراض والدليل عليه : أن القول بإثبات النبوة ، فرع على إثبات حدوث العالم وهذه القاعدة لا يمكن تقريرها ، إلا إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى خصص إحداث العالم بوقت معين ، لا لمخصص ولا لمرجح البتة . وهذا يقتضي القطع بأن الله تعالى قد يفعل الفعل ، لا لغرض ولا لداع أصلاً . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون خلق المعجزات من هذا الباب ؟ وعلى هذا التقدير ، لا يدل المعجز على التصديق ، فنفتقر ههنا إلى بيان أن تخصيص إحداث العالم بالوقت المعين ، لا يمكن أن يكون معللاً بشيء من الأغراض والدواعي . والدليل عليه : أن اختصاص ذلك الوقت بذلك الغرض المعين. إما أن يكون لذاته أو لغيره . ولا جائز أن يكون لذاته . لأنه لو جاز أن يختص ذلك الوقت بذلك الغرض المعين لذاته ، فحينئذ لم يبعد أن يختص ذلك الوقت بسائر الآثار المخصوصة به وإذا جاز ذلك لم يمتنع أن يقال : المقتضي لحدوث العالم في ذلك الوقت هو نفس ذلك الوقت . وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه لا يمكن الاستدلال بحدوث العالم على وجود الصانع . وأما إن كان اختصاص ذلك الوقت بتلك الخاصية ليس لذاته بل لأجل أن فاعل العالم ، خصص ذلك الوقت بذلك الغرض ، وبتلك المصلحة ، عاد الكلام في تخصيص ذلك الوقت بذلك الغرض . ويلزم التسلل ، وهو باطل . ولما بطل هذان القسمان ، ثبت : أن القول بحدوث العالم ، لا يتم لنا إلا إذا قلنا : إن الفاعل المختار يفعل الفعل لا لغرض ولا مرجح أصلا . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فحينئذ يمتنع القطع بأنه تعالى إنما خلق هذا المعجز ، في هذا الوقت لغرض التصديق.
The third objection: Suppose that the actions of God Most High and His judgments may be motivated by motives and purposes, yet they may also not be motivated by motives and purposes. The proof of this is that the assertion of establishing prophethood is a branch of establishing the origination of the world, and this principle cannot be established unless we say that He Most High specified the bringing about of the world at a particular time, with no particularizer and no preference at all. This necessitates the definite conclusion that God Most High may do an action with no purpose and no motive whatsoever. If this is established, then why is it not permissible that the creation of miracles be of this kind? On this assumption, the miracle does not indicate authentication, so we need here to clarify that the specification of the bringing about of the world at the particular time cannot be motivated by any purpose or motive. The proof of this is that the special characteristic of that time with that particular purpose either is due to its essence or due to something else. It cannot be due to its essence, because if it were permissible for that time to have that particular purpose by its essence, then it would not be improbable for that time to have other specific effects; and if that is permissible, then it is not impossible to say that the requisite for the occurrence of the world at that time is that very time. On this assumption, it would be impossible to infer the existence of the Maker from the occurrence of the world. But if the specification of that time with that property is not due to its essence but rather because the Maker of the world specified that time with that purpose and that benefit, then the argument returns to the specification of that time with that purpose, and infinite regress (al-tasalsul) follows, which is invalid. Since both possibilities are invalid, it is established that the statement of the origination of the world is only complete for us if we say that the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) does an action with no purpose and no preference at all. If this is established, then it is impossible to be certain that He Most High only created this miracle at this time for the purpose of authentication.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection (الشبهة الثالثة), which is more elaborate. The objection begins by granting (هب) that some of God's actions may be motivated by motives (دواعي) and purposes (أغراض), but argues that it is also possible that some are not. The proof of this possibility is that the proof of prophethood depends on proving the origination of the world (حدوث العالم). The standard argument for the world's origination requires that God specified the time of creation without any particular reason (لا لمخصص ولا لمرجح). This implies that God can act without any purpose or motive. If so, why cannot the creation of miracles be similarly without purpose? If miracles are purposeless, they do not indicate authentication. The objection then defends the claim that specification of the world's creation time cannot be for a purpose. It presents a dilemma: the special property of that time either inheres in the time itself (لذاته) or comes from an external cause. If it inheres in the time itself, then the time would be the cause of the world's existence, which undermines the proof of a Creator. If it comes from an external cause (God), then that cause's specification would itself need a reason, leading to infinite regress (التسلل), which is invalid. Therefore, the only consistent way to prove the world's origination is to assert that the free agent (الفاعل المختار) acts without purpose or preference. Given that, it cannot be definitively said that God created this miracle specifically for authentication. The objection challenges the necessity of connecting miracles to authentication.

Key Terms
الشبهة الثالثة
دواعي
أغراض
حدوث العالم
فاعل مختار
التسلل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objection denies that God ever acts with purpose, or that it claims all miracles are necessarily without purpose.
Correction: The objection grants that some acts may have purpose but shows that the proof of origination forces admitting purposeless acts, which makes it possible that miracles are purposeless, breaking the necessary link to authentication.

Checkpoint
According to the third objection, why does the proof of the world's origination require that God acts without purpose?
A) Because purposes cannot be attributed to God.
B) Because if the time of creation had a purpose, either the time itself or an external cause would lead to infinite regress or negate the proof of a Creator.
C) Because the world's origination is self-evident.
D) Because prophets must be authenticated by miracles.

Key Takeaway
The third objection undermines the necessary link between miracles and authentication by showing that the proof of the world's origination forces the admission of purposeless divine acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This objection forces you to accept that to prove prophethood you must admit that God can act without any purpose you can understand. Do you truly submit to the fact that many events in your life—and in the world—may have no purpose you will ever know? Or do you secretly resent that uncertainty and demand explanations from God? What would change in your heart if you fully embraced that some divine acts are beyond your comprehension?
?
The objector says that the time of creation was specified without any reason. When you reflect on your own existence in this specific time and place, can you accept that you were placed here without any reason you can grasp? How does that humbling thought affect your reliance on your own understanding? Will you trust that God's wisdom is still at work even when you see no purpose?
?
If miracles can be purposeless, then your reason for following a prophet might rest on something that could be an arbitrary divine act. Does that disturb you, or does it drive you to seek a deeper certainty beyond rational proofs? What is that certainty, and how do you cultivate it in your daily life?

The Fourth Objection: Multiple Possible Purposes for a Miracle
The fourth objection questions the exclusivity of authentication as the purpose of a miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against the evidential value of miracles: that a miracle could have multiple possible divine purposes, not just authentication.

Core Idea
A miracle may serve many divine purposes, so we cannot be sure it authenticates the claimant.

This lesson examines a key objection to using miracles as proof of prophethood. The objector grants that God has purposes in every act, but questions how we can know that the purpose of a miracle is exclusively to authenticate the claimant. Since divine wisdom is vast, many purposes are possible, undermining the certainty of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection begins by conceding a premise (that God has specific purposes) and then poses a question.
The objection does not deny that God has purposes; it questions our ability to know them.
2
Identify the granted premise
The objection assumes that God must have a specific purpose (غرض) and wisdom (حكمة) in every act. This is a concession to the opponent.
3
Understand the core challenge
The key question is: 'What is the proof that the sole purpose of this miracle is to authenticate the claimant?' The objection argues that because purposes are many and God's wisdom is vast, we cannot isolate one purpose.
This challenges the exclusivity of authentication as the purpose.
4
Reflect on the implication
If a miracle could have multiple purposes, then its occurrence does not necessarily confirm the claim of prophethood. The objector uses this to weaken the evidential link.
This is a critical objection that must be answered by proponents of the miracle argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : هب أنه لا بد لله تعالى في كل فعل من غرض معين، حكمة معينة، فما الدليل على أنه لا غرض الله تعالى من فعل هذا المعجز إلا تصديق هذا المدعي ؟ فإن في الأغراض كثرة. وأقسام حكمة الله تعالى في تدبير هذا العالم لا محيط بها أحد البشر.
The fourth objection: Suppose that God Most High must have a specific purpose, a specific wisdom, in every act. Then what is the proof that the purpose of God Most High in performing this miracle is nothing other than authenticating this claimant? For purposes are many, and the categories of the wisdom of God Most High in the administration of this world are beyond the comprehension of any human being.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection (الشبهة الرابعة). It grants the premise that God must have a purpose (غرض) and wisdom (حكمة) in every act. However, the objection questions how we can be certain that the only purpose of a given miracle is to authenticate the claimant (تصديق المدعي). Since divine purposes are numerous and the full scope of God's wisdom in managing the world is beyond human comprehension, there could be many other reasons for the miracle. The objection thus challenges the ability to single out authentication as the sole purpose, thereby weakening the evidential force of the miracle for prophethood.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection argues that because divine purposes are numerous and beyond human comprehension, a miracle alone cannot be taken as certain proof of a claimant's truthfulness.

First Alternative Explanation: Miracle as Beginning of a Custom
Author proposes miracle as first instance of a rare custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's first possible explanation for miracles: that an extraordinary event could be the first occurrence of a rare natural custom, not necessarily a divine sign.

Core Idea
A miracle might be the first instance of a very rare natural pattern.

This lesson explores the first of several alternative explanations proposed by the author. After raising objections, he offers a possibility: an extraordinary event might simply be the start of a new natural custom (عادة) that recurs at long intervals. This challenges the assumption that every miracle must be a direct divine authentication.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Pay attention to the logical flow from the necessity of a first occurrence to the possibility of a rare custom.
The passage cuts off at the end but the main argument is complete.
2
Identify the logical chain
Trace the argument: customary events must have a beginning → otherwise infinite regress → implies eternity of world → undermines free agent and prophethood → therefore events have a first instance → a miracle could be that first instance of a new custom.
3
Examine the counterexample and response
The author anticipates an objection: if the event never recurs, it cannot be a custom. He responds with examples of customs with varying intervals (summer yearly, planetary conjunction every 20 years). Notice how this refutes the objection.
The example 'قران العلويين' refers to the conjunction of Jupiter and Saturn.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this explanation differs from the standard view of miracles as direct divine signs. Why might someone propose such an alternative?
This is part of a larger discussion about the criteria for miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتمال الأول : إنه لا شك أن هذه الحوادث المعتادة منتهية إلى أول . وإلا لزم القول بحدوث حوادث لا أول لها ، وذلك يوجب قدم العالم وقدم العالم يقدح في إثبات الفاعل المختار ، والقدح في الفاعل المختار يمنع من القول بصحة النبوة . فثبت : أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بوجوب انتهاء هذه الحوادث المعتادة إلى أول ومبدأ . فهذا النوع من الحوادث ابتدا في ذلك الوقت ، ثم استمر بعده على نسق معلوم. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لعل هذا الذي حدث الآن ابتداء عادة ستصير عادة مستمرة ، بعد ذلك . فإن قالوا : لما شاهدنا أن هذا الشيء لم يحدث بعد ذلك ، علمنا أنه ليس حدوثه لأجل أنه ابتداء عادة قلنا : العادات قد تكون متكررة في أزمنة متقاربة وقد تكون متكررة في أزمنة متباعدة . مثل : إن العادة جارية بحدوث الصيف في كل سنة مرة واحدة ، وبحدوث قران العلويين في كل عشرين سنة مرة واحدة . فلم يلزم من
The first possibility: There is no doubt that these habitual occurrences have a beginning. Otherwise, one would have to say that there are occurrences with no beginning, and that would necessitate the eternity of the world, and the eternity of the world undermines the proof of the free agent, and undermining the free agent prevents saying the correctness of prophethood. So it is established that one must acknowledge the necessity of these habitual occurrences ending at a beginning and a start. This type of occurrence began at that time and then continued afterwards in a known pattern. If this is established, we say: Perhaps this thing that happened now is the beginning of a custom (ʿādah) that will become continuous after that. If they say: Since we observed that this thing did not happen after that, we know that its occurrence was not because it was the beginning of a custom, we would reply: Customs may be repeated in close times or in distant times. For example, the custom is that summer occurs once every year, and the conjunction of the two superior planets occurs once every twenty years. So it does not follow from
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the first possible alternative explanation (الاحتمال الأول) for a miracle, offered by the author after listing the objections. The argument begins by noting that ordinary customary events (الحوادث المعتادة) must have a first occurrence, otherwise one would have to accept an infinite regress of events with no beginning, which would imply the eternity of the world—a position that undermines the proof of a free agent (الفاعل المختار) and thus also undermines the proof of prophethood. So events must have an initial instance. Given that a miracle is an unusual event, it could simply be the first occurrence of a new habitual pattern (عادة) that will later become regular. The objection that if the event never recurs it cannot be the beginning of a custom is countered by noting that customs can recur at varying intervals—some yearly (like summer) and some much longer (like the conjunction of Jupiter and Saturn every 20 years). Thus a single event might still be the first instance of a very long-period custom, not necessarily a miracle for authentication. The passage is incomplete but the core idea is clear.

الاحتمال الأول
حوادث معتادة
عادة
قران العلويين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This explanation denies that miracles can be divine signs.
Correction: It does not deny that miracles can be signs; it suggests that an extraordinary event might simply be a rare natural occurrence, not necessarily a divine authentication.

Checkpoint

A) It must be a genuine miracle.
B) It could be the first occurrence of a custom that recurs very rarely, like a planetary conjunction every 20 years.
C) It is proof of prophethood.
D) It is impossible for a one-time event to be a custom.

Key Takeaway
An extraordinary event could be the first occurrence of a very rare natural custom, rather than a divine authentication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

First and Second Possibilities: The Role of Custom and Repetition
The author enumerates the first two skeptical explanations of miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two possibilities that skeptics might use to explain away a miracle: that it is either a first-time custom or a repetition of a very long-period custom.

Core Idea
Miracles could be just rare natural events, not divine acts.

The author begins by considering the empirical basis of miracles. If an event has never occurred before, it might be a first-time custom. Alternatively, if it recurs after a very long interval, it could be a repetition of a distant custom. These possibilities challenge the assumption that a miracle is a unique divine intervention.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text for the first two possibilities. Notice the fragmentary nature of the first one.
The first possibility is incomplete due to page break.
2
Understand the first possibility
The first possibility suggests that the event might be a first-time custom – i.e., it occurs for the first time and thus appears miraculous but is actually a new natural pattern.
3
Understand the second possibility
The second possibility gives an example of the fixed stars' precession as a natural repetition every 36,000 years. Similarly, a miracle might be a very infrequent natural event.
This ties to the concept of 'takrir 'ada mutaba'ida'.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Consider how these possibilities undermine the argument that a miracle is a direct sign from God. They offer naturalistic explanations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدم حدوث مثل هذا الحادث بعد هذه المدة، أن لا يكون حدوثه لأجل إنه ابتداء عادة؟ الاحتمال الثاني : لعله حدث لأجل أنه تكرير عادة متباعدة . مثل : ما ضربنا من الأمثلة ، ومثل : أن الكواكب الثابتة تنتهي إلى أول برج الحمل في كل ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة مرة واحدة. فلعل هذا الحادث الذي حدث ، إنما حدث لأنه تكرير عادة متطاولة متباعدة. الاحتمال الثالث : لعله إنما حدث معجزة لنبي آخر ، أو كرامة لولي آخر ، في طرف آخر من أطراف العالم . فاتفق كلام هذا الكاذب ودعواه في ذلك الوقت ، فحدث هذا المعجز على وفق دعواه ، لهذا السبب. الاحتمال الرابع : قد ثبت أنه تعالى قد يقوي الشبهة في بعض المواضع أن المكلف إذا احترز عن تلك الشبهة القوية بالكد العظيم والعناء الشديد ، استوجب الثناء العظيم. فههنا إذا خلق الله تعالى هذا المعجز عقيب دعوى ذلك الشخص، أوهم حدوثه عقيب دعواه إنه إنما حدث تصديقاً له في دعواه . لكن العاقل لما علم أن ذلك وإن كان موهماً . لكنه غير موجب للتصديق ] لاحتمال أنه تعالى إنما أحدث ذلك المعجز ، عقيب دعواه تشديداً على المكلف وتقوية للشبهة عليه . فإذا عرف هذا الاحتمال ، ولم يحمله على التصديق استحق به مزيد الثواب ، حيث احترز عن هذه الشبهة القوية فيكون المقصود من إظهار المعجز ذلك . ألا ترى أن الله تعالى أنزل المتشابهات الكثيرة في كتابه ، ولا مقصود منها سوى ما ذكرناه . فكذا ههنا. الاحتمال الخامس : لعل هذا النبي كان قد علم ، إما بواسطة علم النجوم ، أو بواسطة علم الرمل أو بواسطة تعبير الرؤيا أن الحادث الفلاني ، الخارق للعادة ، سيحدث في اليوم الفلاني ، والناس كانوا غافلين ثم إنه في ذلك اليوم ادعى النبوة ، وادعى حدوث ذلك الحادث المعجز . فلما حدث على وفق دعواه ، ظن الناس أنه إنما عرفه بإلهام الله تعالى وإخباره . الاحتمال السادس : لعلّه تعالى إنما خلق ذلك المعجز في ذلك الوقت لأنه تعالى إن خلق ذلك المعجز ، في ذلك الوقت كان ذلك لطفاً في حق بعض المكلفين، في الدعاء إلى فعل بعض الواجبات العقلية ، وترك بعض المقبحات العقلية. الاحتمال السابع : أن نقول : إن السائل لا يجب عليه تعديد الاحتمالات وتفصيلها ، بل يكفيه أن يقول : لم قلتم : إنه لا حكمة الله تعالى في خلق هذا المعجز ، عقيب دعوى هذا المدعي إلا تصديقه ؟ وعلى المستدل إقامة الدلالة على نفي سائر الاحتمالات فإذا لم يقدر عليه بطل دليله . فههنا يجب على المستدل : إقامة الدلالة على نفي سائر الاحتمالات.
The non-occurrence of such an event after this period, that its occurrence is not because it is a first-time custom? The second possibility: perhaps it occurred because it is a repetition of a distant custom. Like the examples we gave, and like that the fixed stars reach the first degree of Aries once every thirty-six thousand years. So perhaps this event that occurred occurred only because it is a repetition of a long, distant custom. The third possibility: perhaps it occurred as a miracle for another prophet, or a miracle (karama) for another saint, in another part of the world. Then the speech of this liar and his claim at that time coincided, so this miracle occurred in accordance with his claim, for this reason. The fourth possibility: it has been established that He, exalted, may strengthen the doubt in some places so that if the morally responsible person guards against that strong doubt with great effort and severe difficulty, he deserves great praise. So here, if Allah creates this miracle after the claim of that person, it gives the impression that its occurrence after his claim is only to confirm his claim. But the intelligent person, since he knows that although it is suggestive, it does not necessitate belief, due to the possibility that He, exalted, only created that miracle after his claim as a hardship for the morally responsible person and a strengthening of the doubt upon him. So if he recognizes this possibility and does not take it as confirmation, he deserves thereby increased reward, since he guarded against this strong doubt, and the purpose of showing the miracle is that. Do you not see that Allah sent down many ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) in His book, and there is no purpose from them except what we have mentioned. So likewise here. The fifth possibility: perhaps this prophet knew, either through the science of astrology, or through the science of geomancy, or through the interpretation of dreams, that a certain event, breaking the custom, would occur on a certain day, while people were unaware. Then on that day he claimed prophethood and claimed the occurrence of that miraculous event. So when it occurred in accordance with his claim, people thought that he only knew it through Allah's inspiration and informing. The sixth possibility: perhaps He, exalted, only created that miracle at that time because if He created that miracle at that time, it was a kindness (lutf) for some morally responsible persons, to call them to perform certain rational duties and abandon certain rational evil acts. The seventh possibility: that we say: The questioner is not obliged to enumerate the possibilities in detail; rather it suffices for him to say: Why do you say that the wisdom of Allah in creating this miracle after the claim of this claimant is nothing but confirming him? And the arguer must provide evidence to deny all other possibilities. If he is unable to do so, his proof is invalid. So here, the arguer must provide evidence to deny all other possibilities.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage enumerates seven possibilities that could explain the occurrence of a miracle at the time of a claimant to prophethood, thereby undermining the argument that the miracle confirms his truthfulness. The passage begins with a fragment that appears to be the end of the first possibility (the non-recurrence of such an event over time suggests it is a first-time custom; but the argument is incomplete). The second possibility is that the event is a repetition of a very long-period custom, like the precession of the fixed stars. The third possibility is that it is actually a miracle for another prophet or a saint that coincidentally occurred at the time of the false claimant's speech. The fourth possibility is that God intentionally creates the miracle after the claim to test the believer, making it a strong doubt that only the discerning will resist, thereby earning greater reward; the author analogizes this to the ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) in the Quran. The fifth possibility is that the prophet could have known about the future event through astrology, geomancy, or dream interpretation, so his prediction came from those sources rather than divine revelation. The sixth possibility is that God created the miracle at that time as a kindness (lutf) to guide people to rational duties. The seventh possibility is a methodological objection: the questioner does not need to list all possibilities but can simply ask why the miracle must be for confirmation, and the burden of proof is on the arguer to rule out all other possibilities. The passage concludes that the arguer must provide evidence negating all other possibilities; otherwise his proof fails.

Key Terms
عادة
تكرير عادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second possibility, what example is given of a distant custom?
A) The rising of the sun
B) The fixed stars reaching the first degree of Aries every 36,000 years
C) The occurrence of a miracle for a false prophet
D) The repetition of a daily custom

Key Takeaway
The first two possibilities show that extraordinary events can be explained as rare natural phenomena, not necessarily divine acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something extraordinary happens in your life, do you immediately try to find a natural explanation? How might that be a form of intellectual pride that distances you from God?
?
The second possibility suggests that extremely rare events could still be natural. Has there been a blessing you dismissed as 'just a coincidence' that, upon reflection, might have been a sign? What would change if you started viewing such events with reverence?
?
If you believe that every event is either natural or a miracle, how do you decide which is which? Are you afraid of being deceived, or afraid of missing God's intervention? Which fear is more harmful to your faith?

Prophethood

Third and Fourth Possibilities: Coincidence with Another Miracle or Divine Test
The author presents the third and fourth skeptical possibilities concerning miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third and fourth possibilities that undermine a miracle's evidentiary value: coincidence with another miracle/karama, and the possibility that God creates the miracle as a test to strengthen doubt.

Core Idea
A miracle might be coincidental or a divine test, not a confirmation.

The author continues with two more powerful skeptical challenges. The third possibility suggests that the alleged miracle might actually be a miracle for another prophet or a saint that coincidentally aligns with the false claimant's speech. The fourth possibility introduces a theological twist: God might intentionally create the miracle after a false claim to test believers, making it a strong doubt that they must resist.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic for possibilities 3 and 4
Read the passage for the third and fourth possibilities. Note the analogy to the ambiguous verses of the Quran.
2
Understand the third possibility
A miracle could be for another prophet or saint (karama) happening elsewhere at the same time, coinciding with the false claimant's declaration.
This highlights the need to verify the timing and context.
3
Understand the fourth possibility
God may create a miracle after a false claim to test the believer, making it a 'shubha' (doubt) that only the intelligent will overcome, earning greater reward.
This is analogous to the mutashabihat in the Quran.
4
Reflect on the implications
These possibilities show that a miracle's occurrence does not necessarily indicate divine endorsement of the claimant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you were given a powerful spiritual experience that seemed to validate your path, would you automatically trust it, or would you rigorously examine it for the possibility that it is a test? How would you guard your heart against automatic assumption?
?
The author compares this test to ambiguous Quranic verses. When you encounter a difficult verse that challenges your understanding, do you feel frustrated or do you embrace it as an opportunity to earn reward through diligent study? What would you be willing to give up to earn that reward?

Fifth and Sixth Possibilities: Supernatural Knowledge or Divine Kindness
The author presents the fifth and sixth skeptical possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth and sixth possibilities that undermine miracles: the claimant could have prior knowledge through occult sciences, or God could create the miracle as a kindness to guide people.

Core Idea
A miracle could be based on foreknowledge or be a divine kindness, not a proof of prophethood.

The author continues with two more possibilities. The fifth suggests that the prophet might have learned about the future event through astrology, geomancy, or dream interpretation, not through divine revelation. The sixth suggests that God created the miracle at that time as a kindness (lutf) to encourage people to perform rational duties and avoid evils.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic for possibilities 5 and 6
Read the passage for the fifth and sixth possibilities.
2
Understand the fifth possibility
The claimant could have predicted the event through astrology, geomancy, or dream interpretation, giving the impression of a miracle.
3
Understand the sixth possibility
God may create a miracle as a kindness to guide people to right actions, not to confirm a particular prophet.
This links to the concept of lutf.
4
Compare and contrast
Both possibilities offer alternative explanations that do not require the claimant to be truthful.

But the intelligent person, since he knows that although it is suggestive, it does not necessitate belief, due to the possibility that He, exalted, only created that miracle after his claim as a hardship for the morally responsible person and a strengthening of the doubt upon him. So if he recognizes this possibility and does not take it as confirmation, he deserves thereby increased reward, since he guarded against this strong doubt, and the purpose of showing the miracle is that. Do you not see that Allah sent down many ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) in His book, and there is no purpose from them except what we have mentioned. So likewise here. The fifth possibility: perhaps this prophet knew, either through the science of astrology, or through the science of geomancy, or through the interpretation of dreams, that a certain event, breaking the custom, would occur on a certain day, while people were unaware. Then on that day he claimed prophethood and claimed the occurrence of that miraculous event. So when it occurred in accordance with his claim, people thought that he only knew it through Allah's inspiration and informing. The sixth possibility: perhaps He, exalted, only created that miracle at that time because if He created that miracle at that time, it was a kindness (lutf) for some morally responsible persons, to call them to perform certain rational duties and abandon certain rational evil acts. The seventh possibility: that we say: The questioner is not obliged to enumerate the possibilities in detail; rather it suffices for him to say: Why do you say that the wisdom of Allah in creating this miracle after the claim of this claimant is nothing but confirming him? And the arguer must provide evidence to deny all other possibilities. If he is unable to do so, his proof is invalid. So here, the arguer must provide evidence to deny all other possibilities.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage enumerates seven possibilities that could explain the occurrence of a miracle at the time of a claimant to prophethood, thereby undermining the argument that the miracle confirms his truthfulness. The passage begins with a fragment that appears to be the end of the first possibility (the non-recurrence of such an event over time suggests it is a first-time custom; but the argument is incomplete). The second possibility is that the event is a repetition of a very long-period custom, like the precession of the fixed stars. The third possibility is that it is actually a miracle for another prophet or a saint that coincidentally occurred at the time of the false claimant's speech. The fourth possibility is that God intentionally creates the miracle after the claim to test the believer, making it a strong doubt that only the discerning will resist, thereby earning greater reward; the author analogizes this to the ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) in the Quran. The fifth possibility is that the prophet could have known about the future event through astrology, geomancy, or dream interpretation, so his prediction came from those sources rather than divine revelation. The sixth possibility is that God created the miracle at that time as a kindness (lutf) to guide people to rational duties. The seventh possibility is a methodological objection: the questioner does not need to list all possibilities but can simply ask why the miracle must be for confirmation, and the burden of proof is on the arguer to rule out all other possibilities. The passage concludes that the arguer must provide evidence negating all other possibilities; otherwise his proof fails.

Key Takeaway
The fifth and sixth possibilities show that a miracle may be based on prior knowledge or intended as a general kindness, not as a confirmation of a specific prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Seventh Possibility: The Methodological Challenge
The author presents the seventh possibility as a methodological objection regarding the burden of proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh possibility, which is a methodological argument: the burden of proof is on the arguer to rule out all alternative explanations, not on the skeptic to enumerate them.

Core Idea
The skeptic can simply demand proof that the miracle uniquely confirms the prophet; the arguer must eliminate other possibilities.

The final possibility shifts from specific explanations to a meta-argument. The author argues that the skeptic does not need to list all possible alternatives; instead, he can simply ask why the miracle must be for confirmation. The burden of proof falls on the one who claims the miracle proves prophethood to negate all other possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic for the seventh possibility
Read the passage for the seventh possibility. Notice the shift in tone.
2
Understand the methodological point
The skeptic questions why we must assume the miracle is for confirmation; the arguer must provide evidence to exclude all other possibilities.
This is a common argument in debates about miracles.
3
Reflect on the consequence
If the arguer cannot rule out other possibilities, his proof fails. This puts the burden squarely on the proponent.

Objection and Response on the Implication of Doubt
The opponent argues that creating doubt is evil; the author responds with two proofs from scripture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opponent's argument that creating doubt is evil fails, and how the author responds using the example of ambiguous Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Plausible doubt is not evil; the Quran itself contains ambiguous verses.

The opponent raises a powerful objection: even if the miracle could have another purpose, the strong doubt it creates is evil. How can a just God do that? The author rejects this premise and offers two scriptural proofs. The first proof is complete in this passage, while the second is cut short. Let's examine the argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Follow the opponent's logic: if God creates a miracle that strongly suggests confirmation but has another purpose, that casts doubt, and casting doubt is evil.
The opponent assumes that any suggestion of doubt, even if plausible, is evil.
2
Identify the author's response
The author denies the premise that casting plausible doubt is evil. He does not accept the general rule.
This is a key move: the objection's foundation is rejected.
3
Study the first proof
The author points to the ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) in the Quran. They suggest false interpretations but are not evil because they are plausible and admit correct interpretation.
This is an analogy: if ambiguous verses are not evil, then plausible doubt from miracles is also not evil.
4
Reflect on the second proof
The author begins a second proof about Iblis and whisperings, but the passage cuts off. Consider how this also illustrates that plausible doubt is not inherently evil.
The argument is incomplete, but the direction is clear: God allows Iblis to whisper, yet that is not evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : هب أنه لا يتعين هذا الفرض، لأنه ظاهر الاحتمال ، فلو خلق الله تعالى ذلك المعجز . لغرض آخر ، مع أنه يوهم التصديق إيهاماً قوياً ، لكان ذلك إلقاء للشبهة [ في العقول . وإنه قبيح. قلنا : لا نسلم أن إلقاء الشبهة المحتملة قبيح . والذي يدل عليه الأول : إنه تعالى أنزل المتشابهات الكثيرة ، ولا شك أنها توهم الأباطيل . إلا أنها لما كانت محتملة ، لم تقبح . فكذا ههنا الثاني : إنه تعالى أبقى ( إبليس ) وجنوده ، وهم يسعون في الوساوس
If they say: Suppose this assumption is not determined, because it is apparently possible, so if Allah created that miracle for another purpose, while it strongly suggests confirmation, that would be casting doubt into minds, and it is evil. We say: We do not accept that casting plausible doubt is evil. What indicates it: First: He, exalted, sent down many ambiguous verses (mutashabihat), and there is no doubt that they suggest falsehoods. But because they are plausible, they are not evil. So likewise here. Second: He, exalted, left Iblis and his soldiers, and they strive in whisperings (wasawis)...
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and the author's response. The opponent argues that even if the fourth possibility (that God creates the miracle to test believers) is plausible, it still creates strong doubt about the miracle's purpose, and casting such doubt into minds is evil. The author rejects the premise that casting plausible doubt is evil. He gives two proofs: (1) God revealed many ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) in the Quran that suggest false interpretations, but because they are plausible (i.e., admit of correct interpretation), they are not evil. The same applies here. (2) God has left Iblis and his armies to whisper evil suggestions, which also create doubt, yet this is not considered evil because it is part of divine wisdom. The second argument is incomplete due to the page ending, but the point is that the existence of plausible doubt is not inherently evil.

Miracles as Divine Affirmation: The Kingly Analogy
An analogy from a king's public act to argue that miracles confirm a prophet's truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an analogy from a king's public act is used to argue that miracles confirm a prophet's truthfulness, and the initial objection that mere conjunction does not imply causation.

Core Idea
Miracles are like a king's public affirmation: they confirm the prophet's truthfulness.

This passage presents a powerful analogy from everyday life to argue that miracles serve as divine verification of prophethood. The author compares the situation to a king who performs an unusual act in response to a claimant, which the people interpret as affirmation. Then he introduces a key objection about the relationship between cause and effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy from kings
Carefully read the analogy: a king in a grand assembly, a claimant asking for a sign, and the king's unusual act. Notice how the people infer the claimant's truthfulness.
This analogy is the foundation of the argument.
2
Apply to the unseen realm
The author then applies this logic to the divine: if such an act confirms truthfulness in the witnessed realm, then a miracle (an unusual act by God) should confirm the prophet's truthfulness in the unseen realm.
3
Identify the objection
The objection states that the mere conjunction of the king's act with the claimant's request does not prove causation or affirmation. Think about why correlation is not enough.
The author promises to elaborate on this objection in more detail later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن حكاية حائل من استحل بظهور المعوز على صدق المحمي قالوا : إن الملك العظيم ، إذا جلس في المحفل العظيم . ثم قام واحد القوم ، وقال : يا أيها الناس : إني رسول هذا الملك إليكم . ثم قال : يا أيها الملك إن كنت صادقاً في هذا القول، فخالف عـادتـك ، وقم من سرير مملكتك . فإذا رأى الناس أن ذلك الملك ، أتى بذلك الفعل الذي التمسه ذلك المدعي علموا قطعاً : أنه إنما فعل ذلك تصديقاً لذلك المدعي . وإذا ثبت هذا في الشاهد، وجب أن يكون الحال كذلك في الغائب . فيثبت : أن خلق المعجز يدل على التصديق . والاعتراض عليه من وجهين : الأول : إن حصول ذلك الفعل لا يدل على التصديق (۱) وبيانه : أن القدر المعلوم هو أنه حصل ذلك الفعل ، مقارناً لذلك الطلب ، إلا أن حصول الشيء مع الشيء ، لا يدل على العلية ، لا قطعاً ولا ظاهراً . ونحن نبين هذا القول تارة بطريق الإجمال، وتارة بطريق التفصيل . (۱) سقط (ت).
Chapter Eight: Averting Objection by Manifesting a Miracle to Verify the Truthfulness of the Claimant. They said: When a great king sits in a great assembly, then a man among the people stands up and says: 'O people, I am the messenger of this king to you.' Then he says: 'O king, if you are truthful in this claim, then break your custom and rise from the throne of your kingdom.' So when the people see that the king performed that action which the claimant requested, they know decisively that he only did that in affirmation of that claimant. And if this is established in the witnessed, then it must be the same in the unseen. So it is established that creating a miracle indicates affirmation. The objection to this is from two aspects: First: That the occurrence of that action does not indicate affirmation. Its explanation: The known fact is that the action occurred concomitant with that request, but the occurrence of a thing with a thing does not indicate causality, neither decisively nor apparently. And we will explain this statement sometimes by way of summary and sometimes by way of detail.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by analogy that miracles confirm a prophet's truthfulness. The analogy: A king in an assembly is asked by a claimant to do an unusual act (rising from his throne). If the king does it, everyone knows he is affirming the claimant. So in the unseen, when God creates a miracle at the request of a prophet, that indicates God's affirmation of the prophet's truthfulness. Then the author introduces an objection with two aspects; here only the first is begun: that mere conjunction does not prove causation. The author promises to explain further in summary and detail, but the text ends.

Key Terms
شاهد (witnessed/realm of observation)
غائب (unseen/realm of the divine)
معجز (miracle)
تصديق (affirmation/verification)
علية (causality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy is about proving God's existence or power.
Correction: The analogy is specifically about using miracles to authenticate prophethood, not to prove God's existence or attributes.

Checkpoint
What does the king's act of rising from his throne in the analogy demonstrate?
A) That the king is powerful.
B) That the claimant is truthful.
C) That the king is following his custom.
D) That the people should fear the king.

Key Takeaway
The analogy from the king's act illustrates how miracles are taken as divine verification, but the objection raises the issue that mere correlation does not prove causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about a miracle attributed to a prophet or saint, do you automatically assume it proves their truthfulness? How does the objection in this lesson challenge that assumption? Examine your own heart: are you truly convinced by evidence, or do you accept things out of habit?
?
Think of a time you assumed causation from mere sequence—perhaps in personal prayers or life events. How did that assumption affect your faith? Could you be making the same mistake in how you view miracles? What would it take for you to genuinely verify a claim of prophethood?
?
If you were the king in the analogy, would you rise from your throne to affirm a messenger? Are you willing to 'break your custom'—change your comfortable habits—when God sends a sign to you? What is preventing you from responding with certainty?
