First Difference: The King's Hidden Wisdom
First difference: the king's hidden wisdom makes his actions ambiguous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a king's hidden wisdom prevents his actions from being straightforward indicators of truth or falsehood.

Core Idea
A king's deep wisdom hides benefits, so his actions are not clear signs.

The author begins by describing a king of profound intellect who may perform actions that appear harmful but actually have hidden benefits. Because we attribute such wisdom to him, his outward actions do not directly indicate whether a claimant is truthful. This sets up the first key difference between a worldly king and God, showing why analogy fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of the king
Notice the adjectives used: 'deep understanding', 'great thought', 'perfect intellect'. They establish the king's extraordinary wisdom.
2
Identify the paradox
The king may do actions that seem to cause great evils, but we believe he has hidden beneficial purposes. This makes his actions ambiguous.
3
Connect to the main argument
Because the king's actions are ambiguous, they cannot serve as clear evidence for belief or disbelief. This undermines the analogy with God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنا نعتقد فيه أنه ملك بعيد الغور ، عظيم الفكر ، كامل العقل قد يأتي بأفعال يظن بها أنها توجب المفاسد العظيمة إلا أنه بعقله الكامل ، وفكره الغائص ، يعرف فيها من وجوه المصالح الخفية مالا يقف عليه الغير البتة . فإذا اعتقدنا في الملك هذه الصفة ، لم يصر ظاهر أفعاله دليلاً على التصديق والتكذيب . ومن المعلوم : أن أقسام حكمة الله تعالى في تدبير السموات والأرض ، فما لا سبيل لأحد إلى الوقوف على معاقده وضوابطه ، فكيف يمكن قياس أحد البابين على الآخر ؟
مع
الفرق الثاني : إن الملك في الشاهد لو أتى بذلك المطلوب ، أن ذلك المدعي يكون كاذباً . لكان ذلك سعياً منه في [ إفساد مملكته ، وذلك بعيد . لأن سعيه في إفساد مملكة نفسه ، سعى منه في (۱) ] إلحاق الضرر بنفسه ، وأنه بعيد ، بخلاف الحال في هذه المسألة ، فإنه تعالى لا ينفعه شيء ، ولا يضره شيء . فكيف يمكن قياس أحد البابين على الآخر ؟
الفرق الثالث : إنا قبل ذلك المجلس شاهدنا صورته ، وعرفنا كيفية تدبيره وضبطه للمملكة ، فتتأكد بعض تلك الإمارات بالبعض ، ويتولد من المجموع : الجزم واليقين ، أما في حق الله تعالى فلم يشاهد منه إلا هذا الفعل الواحد ، الدال على صدق هذا المدعي ، وأما البواقي فأحوال عظيمة لا تصل إلى كيفيتها وكميتها عقول الخلق ، فظهر الفرق .
ثم نقول : إن هذه الفروق إنما نحتاج إلى ذكرها ، إذا عرفنا بأن قياس الغائب على الشاهد : طريقة مقبولة في المسائل العقلية . فإنا قد بينا في علم المنطق بالدلائل الكثيرة أنها طريقة ضعيفة لا تفيد الظن المقنع ، فكيف الجزم
واليقين ؟ [ فإنه لا حاجة بنا إلى هذه الفروق ، بل ذكرها يجري مجرى الزيادة ،
التي لا حاجة إليها . والله أعلم (۲) ] .
But we believe concerning him that he is a king of deep understanding, great thought, perfect intellect; he may perform actions that are thought to lead to great evils, but with his perfect intellect and penetrating thought he knows in them hidden beneficial aspects that no one else can ever grasp. So if we hold this attribute of the king, then the outward appearance of his actions does not become evidence for belief or disbelief. And it is known that the divisions of the wisdom of God Almighty in the management of the heavens and the earth, and what no one can grasp of its bases and principles — so how can one of the two domains be measured against the other? Moreover, the second difference: that the king in the visible world, if he had done that required action, then that claimant would be lying. That would be an effort from him to corrupt his kingdom, and that is far-fetched. Because his effort to corrupt his own kingdom is an effort to bring harm to himself, and that is far-fetched, unlike the case in this matter, for He Almighty is not benefited by anything nor harmed by anything. So how can one of the two domains be measured against the other? The third difference: we, before that assembly, have seen his form and known the manner of his management and control of the kingdom, so some of those indications are strengthened by others, and from the totality arises certainty and conviction. But regarding God Almighty, nothing has been seen from Him except this single act that indicates the truth of this claimant; as for the rest, they are tremendous states whose quality and quantity the intellects of creatures cannot reach. So the difference becomes clear. Then we say: we only need to mention these differences if we hold that analogical reasoning from the unseen to the visible is an accepted method in intellectual matters. For we have already shown in the science of logic with abundant proofs that it is a weak method that does not yield persuasive conjecture, let alone certainty and conviction? [For we have no need for these differences; rather their mention is merely an addition that is not needed. And God knows best (2).]
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents three differences (furūq) between the case of a worldly king and the case of God, in order to refute an objection that if a king's actions could be ambiguous, then God's miraculous actions could also be ambiguous. The first difference: the king's hidden wisdom and deep understanding means that his actions are not straightforward indicators of truth or falsehood, because he may have hidden reasons. The second difference: a king would not act against his own interest by supporting a false claimant, as that would harm his kingdom; but God is not affected by benefit or harm, so this argument does not apply. The third difference: we have prior knowledge of the king's character and management from observing him, so we can confirm indications; but for God we only see the miracle itself, without additional context. The author then concludes that mentioning these differences is actually unnecessary because the method of qiyās al-ghā'ib 'alā al-shāhid (analogical reasoning from the visible to the invisible) has already been shown in logic to be weak and not yielding certainty. Thus the whole discussion is a cautionary note; the real refutation is based on the invalidity of such analogy.

Key Terms
بعيد الغور
عظيم الفكر
كامل العقل
المفاسد
المصالح الخفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think that if a king's actions are ambiguous, then God's miracles are also ambiguous, so miracles cannot prove prophethood.
Correction: The author argues that the king's ambiguity is due to his limited wisdom and self-interest, whereas God's wisdom is absolute and not comparable. The analogy does not hold.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the king's hidden wisdom make his actions ambiguous?
A) Because the king is often deceptive.
B) Because we believe he has hidden beneficial aspects that others cannot see.
C) Because the king does not care about the consequences.
D) Because the king's actions are always irrational.

Key Takeaway
Hidden wisdom in a king makes his actions ambiguous, so they cannot be used as clear evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems overwhelmingly negative or unjust, do you immediately assume there is no wisdom in it, or do you pause to consider that you might be missing hidden benefits? How can this lesson train you to react differently starting today?
?
The king's hidden wisdom makes his actions ambiguous. But what about your own life? Are you willing to trust that God's management of your affairs contains hidden benefits even when everything appears harmful? What would change if you truly internalized this?
?
You often judge people and events by their outward appearance. After reading this passage, how will you intentionally refrain from making final judgments based on what you see, and instead commit to waiting for God's hidden wisdom to unfold? Write down one specific situation right now where you need to apply this.

Second Difference: The King's Self-Interest
Second difference: the king's self-interest vs. God's self-sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a king's self-interest prevents him from supporting a false claimant, unlike God who is not affected by benefit or harm.

Core Idea
A king avoids harming his kingdom; God is unaffected by benefit or harm.

The second difference focuses on motivation. A worldly king would not undermine his own kingdom by endorsing a false claimant, because that would harm himself. But God is beyond benefit and harm, so this reasoning does not apply. This further shows the flaw in analogizing from the visible to the invisible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The author imagines a king who, if he performed the required action (supporting a false prophet), would be acting against his own interest. This would be absurd.
2
Understand the difference
For a king, harming his kingdom harms himself. But God is not subject to benefit or harm, so God could permit false miracles without personal loss.
3
Conclude the analogy's failure
The difference in nature (self-interest vs. self-sufficiency) makes the analogy invalid.

Why does the author argue that a king would not support a false claimant?

?
When you face a trial, do you secretly believe God acts out of self-interest like a human king? How does this lesson's truth—that God is neither benefited nor harmed—free you from that assumption? Will you repent of attributing human motives to Him?
?
If God gains nothing from your obedience and loses nothing from your sin, why do you still obey? Does your worship rely on His need or your love? Let this truth expose your heart today.
?
How often do you judge God's actions by human standards, thinking He should act to protect His 'kingdom'? Will you stop measuring His ways against your own limited understanding, trusting His complete self-sufficiency?

Third Difference: Prior Knowledge of the King
Third difference: prior knowledge of the king vs. lack thereof for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how prior observation of the king's character strengthens indications, whereas for God we only see the miracle itself without context.

Core Idea
We know the king from prior evidence; God is known only through the single miracle.

The third difference highlights the role of prior knowledge. In the case of a king, we have seen his character and governance, so multiple indications reinforce each other to yield certainty. But for God, we have no such prior context; the miracle alone is the only evidence. This additional difference further undermines the analogy between visible and invisible.

Guided Path
1
Notice the contrast in context
The author contrasts the wealth of prior information about the king with the single act of the miracle for God.
2
Understand the cumulative effect
Multiple indications from the king's past behavior combine to produce certainty. This is absent in the divine case.
3
Realize the implications
Without prior knowledge, the miracle alone does not suffice to reach the same level of certainty.

Source Text

?
You claim to believe in God based on miracles you've never seen, yet you rely on multiple testimonies for everyday matters like a king's character. Why do you find it harder to trust the single testimony of revelation than the accumulated reports of people?
?
The king's known character gave you confidence; but with God, you only have the miracle. Are you secretly dissatisfied that God hasn't shown you His 'management' and 'control' in a way you can observe? What does that dissatisfaction reveal about your reliance on empirical evidence over faith?
?
If the only evidence you have is the miracle of guidance in your own heart—the single act of faith—is it enough to produce certainty? Or are you waiting for more signs before you fully submit? What would it take for you to commit wholly to what little you have?

Methodological Conclusion: The Weakness of Qiyas
Methodological critique of analogical reasoning from the visible to the invisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author considers the entire discussion of differences unnecessary, given the inherent weakness of analogical reasoning from the visible to the invisible.

Core Idea
Analogy from visible to invisible is inherently weak and cannot yield certainty.

After presenting three differences, the author steps back to make a methodological point. He explains that these differences are only needed if one accepts analogical reasoning (qiyas) as valid. But he argues that this method has already been shown in logic to be weak, producing only persuasive conjecture, not certainty. Therefore, the entire discussion of differences is an extra, not a fundamental refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's condition
Notice the phrase 'if we hold that analogical reasoning is an accepted method'. The author is setting up a conditional.
2
Understand the logical verdict
The author states that in logic it has been proven that qiyas is a weak method, not even yielding persuasive conjecture, let alone certainty.
3
Grasp the overall strategy
The author uses the differences as a supplementary argument, but his primary refutation is the invalidity of qiyas itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنا نعتقد فيه أنه ملك بعيد الغور ، عظيم الفكر ، كامل العقل قد يأتي بأفعال يظن بها أنها توجب المفاسد العظيمة إلا أنه بعقله الكامل ، وفكره الغائص ، يعرف فيها من وجوه المصالح الخفية مالا يقف عليه الغير البتة . فإذا اعتقدنا في الملك هذه الصفة ، لم يصر ظاهر أفعاله دليلاً على التصديق والتكذيب . ومن المعلوم : أن أقسام حكمة الله تعالى في تدبير السموات والأرض ، فما لا سبيل لأحد إلى الوقوف على معاقده وضوابطه ، فكيف يمكن قياس أحد البابين على الآخر ؟
مع
الفرق الثاني : إن الملك في الشاهد لو أتى بذلك المطلوب ، أن ذلك المدعي يكون كاذباً . لكان ذلك سعياً منه في [ إفساد مملكته ، وذلك بعيد . لأن سعيه في إفساد مملكة نفسه ، سعى منه في (۱) ] إلحاق الضرر بنفسه ، وأنه بعيد ، بخلاف الحال في هذه المسألة ، فإنه تعالى لا ينفعه شيء ، ولا يضره شيء . فكيف يمكن قياس أحد البابين على الآخر ؟
الفرق الثالث : إنا قبل ذلك المجلس شاهدنا صورته ، وعرفنا كيفية تدبيره وضبطه للمملكة ، فتتأكد بعض تلك الإمارات بالبعض ، ويتولد من المجموع : الجزم واليقين ، أما في حق الله تعالى فلم يشاهد منه إلا هذا الفعل الواحد ، الدال على صدق هذا المدعي ، وأما البواقي فأحوال عظيمة لا تصل إلى كيفيتها وكميتها عقول الخلق ، فظهر الفرق .
ثم نقول : إن هذه الفروق إنما نحتاج إلى ذكرها ، إذا عرفنا بأن قياس الغائب على الشاهد : طريقة مقبولة في المسائل العقلية . فإنا قد بينا في علم المنطق بالدلائل الكثيرة أنها طريقة ضعيفة لا تفيد الظن المقنع ، فكيف الجزم
واليقين ؟ [ فإنه لا حاجة بنا إلى هذه الفروق ، بل ذكرها يجري مجرى الزيادة ،
التي لا حاجة إليها . والله أعلم (۲) ] .
But we believe concerning him that he is a king of deep understanding, great thought, perfect intellect; he may perform actions that are thought to lead to great evils, but with his perfect intellect and penetrating thought he knows in them hidden beneficial aspects that no one else can ever grasp. So if we hold this attribute of the king, then the outward appearance of his actions does not become evidence for belief or disbelief. And it is known that the divisions of the wisdom of God Almighty in the management of the heavens and the earth, and what no one can grasp of its bases and principles — so how can one of the two domains be measured against the other? Moreover, the second difference: that the king in the visible world, if he had done that required action, then that claimant would be lying. That would be an effort from him to corrupt his kingdom, and that is far-fetched. Because his effort to corrupt his own kingdom is an effort to bring harm to himself, and that is far-fetched, unlike the case in this matter, for He Almighty is not benefited by anything nor harmed by anything. So how can one of the two domains be measured against the other? The third difference: we, before that assembly, have seen his form and known the manner of his management and control of the kingdom, so some of those indications are strengthened by others, and from the totality arises certainty and conviction. But regarding God Almighty, nothing has been seen from Him except this single act that indicates the truth of this claimant; as for the rest, they are tremendous states whose quality and quantity the intellects of creatures cannot reach. So the difference becomes clear. Then we say: we only need to mention these differences if we hold that analogical reasoning from the unseen to the visible is an accepted method in intellectual matters. For we have already shown in the science of logic with abundant proofs that it is a weak method that does not yield persuasive conjecture, let alone certainty and conviction? [For we have no need for these differences; rather their mention is merely an addition that is not needed. And God knows best (2).]
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents three differences (furūq) between the case of a worldly king and the case of God, in order to refute an objection that if a king's actions could be ambiguous, then God's miraculous actions could also be ambiguous. The first difference: the king's hidden wisdom and deep understanding means that his actions are not straightforward indicators of truth or falsehood, because he may have hidden reasons. The second difference: a king would not act against his own interest by supporting a false claimant, as that would harm his kingdom; but God is not affected by benefit or harm, so this argument does not apply. The third difference: we have prior knowledge of the king's character and management from observing him, so we can confirm indications; but for God we only see the miracle itself, without additional context. The author then concludes that mentioning these differences is actually unnecessary because the method of qiyās al-ghā'ib 'alā al-shāhid (analogical reasoning from the visible to the invisible) has already been shown in logic to be weak and not yielding certainty. Thus the whole discussion is a cautionary note; the real refutation is based on the invalidity of such analogy.

Key Terms
قياس الغائب على الشاهد
الظن المقنع
الجزم واليقين
المنطق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some readers might think that the author is providing differences to salvage the analogy, or that he accepts qiyas as valid.
Correction: The author explicitly states that qiyas is a weak method, and the differences are only mentioned conditionally, not as a primary argument.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the three differences are not really needed?
A) Because they are too complicated.
B) Because analogical reasoning from the visible to the invisible has already been proven weak in logic.
C) Because the differences are not convincing.
D) Because the king example is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Analogical reasoning from visible to invisible is fundamentally weak; therefore, detailed comparisons are unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about Allah (سُبْحَانَهُ وَتَعَالَى) based on an analogy from the visible world, without checking whether that analogy is valid for the unseen? What was that belief, and how certain were you?
?
The author says qiyas yields at best persuasive conjecture, never certainty. Think about your own faith: are there areas where you are content with mere conjecture instead of seeking the certainty that comes from revelation? What would it take for you to move from conjecture to certainty?
?
This passage calls into question a whole method of reasoning. Are you willing to let go of arguments you once found persuasive if they are based on weak analogies? What is one such argument you need to re-examine today?

First Doubt: Miracles Indicate Only Probability
First doubt: miracles give only probable indication of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first doubt raised against the proof of prophethood through miracles: that miracles, like spoken claims, only provide probabilistic evidence.

Core Idea
Miracles, like spoken claims, are open to interpretation and thus only probably indicate prophethood.

Sanusi presents the first of several doubts that skeptics raise against using miracles as proof of prophethood. This doubt argues that even if miracles stand in place of a verbal declaration, that declaration itself is not certain but only probable. Understanding this objection is essential before we see how it is refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: the doubt is presented as an objection beginning with 'إن أقصى ما في الباب'.
The passage contains some garbled words in the header, but the main text is clear.
2
Identify the key analogy
The doubt compares a miracle to a spoken claim of prophethood. What is the basis for this comparison? Find where the analogy is made explicit.
Look for the phrase 'قائم مقام التصديق'.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
The doubt argues that since a spoken claim is only probabilistic, the miracle that stands in its place must also be probabilistic. Follow the reasoning step by step.
The conclusion is that the highest level of miracles cannot give certainty.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this doubt is damaging if left unanswered. What would it mean for the proof of prophethood if miracles are only probable?
This sets the stage for the refutation in the following lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التاسع
في
تقرير نوع اخر من الشبهات في بيان ان [؟] خامهر الفصل الخابق الساحة الموافق المسوى مع مجم المعارضة لا يحل على صحة المحمي [؟]

الشبهة الأولى : إن أقصى ما في الباب : أنكم تقولون : إن المعجز قائم مقام التصديق [ بالقول (۱) ] فيقول : هب أنه كذلك إلا أن الرجل إذا قال : فلان رسولي إليكم فهذا كلام يحتمل التأويل، ويمكن صرفه عن ظاهره بالدليل . وإذا كان كذلك لم تكن دلالة هذا القول على ثبوت هذا المعنى دلالة قاطعة ، بل كانت دلالة ظنية . فثبت أن دلالة هذا اللفظ على حصول النبوة دلالة ظنية . وثبت : أن أقصى مراتب المعجزات أن تكون قائمة مقام هذا اللفظ [ على حصول النبوة ) ] فبأن تكون دلالة المعجز على التصديق دلالة ظنية
كان أولى .
Chapter Nine: On presenting another type of doubt, in explaining that [?] ... [?] does not indicate the validity of the claim [?]

The first doubt: The most that can be said is that you say: the miracle stands in the place of verbal affirmation [with speech (1)] and it is said: suppose it is so, except that when a man says: 'So-and-so is my messenger to you,' this speech is open to interpretation and can be diverted from its apparent meaning by evidence. And if so, then the indication of this speech for the existence of this meaning is not a definitive indication, but rather a probabilistic indication. So it is established that the indication of this utterance for the occurrence of prophethood is probabilistic. And it is established that the highest level of miracles is to stand in the place of this utterance [for the occurrence of prophethood (2)] so for the indication of the miracle for truthfulness to be probabilistic is even more fitting.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the first doubt (shubha) against the proof of prophethood by miracles. The doubt is that the miracle is essentially equivalent to a spoken claim of prophethood. But a spoken claim is not decisive because it can be interpreted metaphorically or otherwise diverted from its apparent meaning by evidence. So it only gives probabilistic indication. Since the miracle only stands in the place of such a claim, its indication should also be considered probabilistic, not certain. The objector suggests that to claim certainty from a miracle is therefore unwarranted. The author will presumably refute this doubt in subsequent pages.

Key Terms

يحتمل التأويل
صرفه عن ظاهره
دلالة قاطعة
دلالة ظنية
النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this doubt outright denies that miracles can be evidence at all.
Correction: The doubt does not deny miracles as evidence; it argues that the evidence is probabilistic, not certain. It is an objection that will be addressed.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the first doubt?
A) Miracles are impossible.
B) Miracles provide only probable evidence for prophethood, like a spoken claim.
C) Spoken claims are better than miracles.
D) Prophethood cannot be proven at all.

Key Takeaway
The first doubt challenges the certainty of miracles by comparing them to probabilistic spoken claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever approached the claims of the Qur'an as merely 'probable', leaving room for your own interpretation rather than submitting to its clear command? How does the doubt's argument challenge your level of conviction?
?
The doubt says even a direct statement like 'So-and-so is my messenger' can be interpreted away. When you read the Prophet's ﷺ words, do you treat them as open to reinterpretation, or as decisive evidence that demands obedience? What would it take for you to move from probabilistic acceptance to certainty?
?
If you honestly examine your faith, are there areas where you rely on 'probabilistic' belief in prophethood rather than certainty? What concrete step can you take today to seek the definitive proof that miracles provide?

Miracles and Truth: The Condition of Coexistence
Author clarifies that miracles need a condition for indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why miracles alone do not directly prove truthfulness, and how the principle of coexistence (dawarān) is used to argue for a condition.

Core Idea
A miracle indicates truth only when it coexists with the claim, not by itself.

In this passage, the author defends a nuanced view: miracles are not self-evident proofs of prophethood. Rather, they require an additional condition—coexistence with the claimant's message. He then addresses a logical objection about causality and shows why the argument from simple coincidence is insufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once slowly, then read the literal translation to understand the author's exact words.
Notice the careful qualification: 'We do not claim... rather, we only claim...'
2
Identify the author's core claim
The author states two things: what he does NOT claim (direct indication) and what he DOES claim (indication with a condition). Underline the condition.
The condition is 'كون المعجز دائراً مع تلك الدعوى'.
3
Understand the objection about dawarān
The author brings up the rational principle that coexistence (dawarān) does not give certainty about causality. He notes that scholars disagree on whether it gives probabilistic indication.
This is a key logical debate in uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām.
4
Analyze the conclusion
The author concludes that the path of using mere coexistence to prove causality is invalid. Reflect on why this matters for proving prophethood.
The author implies he has refuted opposing views elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنا لا ندعي أن كون الفعل خارقاً للعادة يدل على الصدق، بل إنما ندعي حصول هذه الدلالة عند حصول شرط زائد، وهو كون ذلك المعجز دائراً مع تلك الدعوى، وجوداً وعدما. إلا أنا نقول: إن العقلاء أطبقوا على أن الدوران مع الشيء لا يفيد العلية إفادة قطعية؟ واختلفوا في أنه هل يدل على حصول العلية دلالة ظنية أم لا؟ وقد بينا هذا المطلوب بالوجوه الكثيرة. فيثبت: أن التمسك بهذا الطريق غير جائز.
We do not claim that an act being extraordinary (khāriq li-l-ʿāda) indicates truthfulness (ṣidq); rather, we only claim that this indication occurs when an additional condition is present, namely that the miracle (muʿjiz) coexists with that claim, in existence and non-existence. But we say: The rational people have unanimously agreed that coexistence (dawarān) with something does not provide certainty (qaṭʿiyya) about causality (ʿilliyya). And they differed on whether it indicates causality with probabilistic indication (dalāla ẓanniyya) or not. And we have explained this claim with many arguments. So it is established that clinging to this path is not permissible.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a preliminary statement about the relationship between miracles and truthfulness. The author clarifies his position: he does not claim that an extraordinary act (muʿjiza) itself directly indicates the truth of the claimant. Rather, he argues that such indication requires an additional condition, namely that the miracle coincides with the claim—both in its occurrence and its absence. He then addresses the logical principle of 'coexistence' (dawarān), noting that rational thinkers agree it does not provide a definitive proof of causality. They only dispute whether it provides probable indication. The author concludes that he has already refuted this approach with many arguments, so relying on this method is invalid. This sets up the subsequent refutations of specific objections.

معجز
خرق العادة
دوران
علية
قطعية
ظنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author completely denies that miracles can indicate truth.
Correction: The author does not deny indication; he requires an additional condition of coexistence.

Checkpoint
What is the additional condition mentioned by the author for a miracle to indicate truth?
A) It must be extraordinary.
B) It must coexist with the claim in existence and non-existence.
C) It must be performed by a prophet.
D) It must be witnessed by many people.

Key Takeaway
Miracles indicate truth only when they coexist with the claim, and coexistence alone does not prove causality definitively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Refuting the Third Objection: Miracles and the Condition of No Opposition
Author refutes the third objection by analyzing the condition of no opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition of no opposition cannot serve as a valid condition for a miracle's indication of truth, and how the author refutes both possible formulations of this condition.

Core Idea
The condition of no opposition is either insufficient or unknowable — making it an invalid basis for miracles.

The third objection against miracles claims that if a miracle indicates truth, it must be because no one can oppose it. But this condition itself is problematic: does 'no opposition' mean at the moment, forever, or something in between? The author examines each possibility and shows that none can work. This lesson walks through that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage to grasp the third objection: that a miracle's indication of truth is either conditioned by no opposition or not, and both are argued to be false.
2
Follow the three divisions of 'no opposition'
Notice how the author splits the conditioned case into three: absence at the moment, absence forever, or an intermediate rank. Each is refuted with specific reasoning.
Temporary absence is insufficient, eternal absence is unknowable, intermediate ranks are arbitrary.
3
Examine the additional arguments
The author adds two more arguments: one about the scope of absence (present people, all people, or intermediate), and one about non-existence being pure negation with no differentiating power.
4
Conclude with the second principal division
Finally, the author declares that the option of 'not conditioned by no opposition' is clearly false. Thus both options fail, so miracles cannot indicate truth on this view.
This is the overall conclusion of the objection, which the author presents as a challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة: دلالة المعجز على الصدق لو حصلت، لكانت إما أن تكون مشروطة بعدم المعارضة، أو لا تكون مشروطة به، والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بدلالة المعجز على الصدق. لوجوه: إنما قلنا: إنه لا يمكن أن تكون هذه الدلالة مشروطة بعدم المعارضة الأول: إنه إما أن يكفي في كون المعجز، معجزاً، عدم المعارضة في الحال، أو المعتبر عدم المعارضة أبداً [أو المعتبر عدم المعارضة في مرتبة متوسطة بين المرتبتين المذكورتين. والأقسام] الثلاثة باطلة. أما عدم المعارضة في الحال، فإنه لا يكفي في كون الفعل معجزاً. فكم من إنسان يأتي بعمل، فلا يقدر الحاضرون في الحال على معارضته، مع أنه لا يكون ذلك العمل معجزاً بالاتفاق. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يكون الشرط في كونه معجزاً عدم المعارضة أبداً فهذا الشرط مجهول. فمن الذي يمكنه أن يعلم أن أحداً من الواردين بعده إلى قيام القيامة، لا يمكنه الإتيان بهذه المعارضة؟ وإذا صار هذا الشرط مجهولاً صار المشروط أيضاً مجهولاً فوجب أن تصير المعجزات بأسرها: مجهولة. وأما القسم الثالث: وهو المرتبة المتوسطة بين المرتبتين المذكورتين. فنقول: إن تلك المراتب المتوسطة كثيرة متفاوتة. وليس اعتبار بعضها أولى من اعتبار البواقي [فكان اشتراط واحدة منها، وإلغاء البواقي] محض التحكم، وهو باطل [فيثبت أن اشتراط عدم المعارضة ينقسم إلى هذه الأقسام الثلاثة، وثبت أنها بأسرها أقسام باطلة، فكان القول باعتبار عدم المعارضة باطلا]. الوجه الثاني في فساد هذا القسم: إن المعتبر عدم المعارضة إما من الحاضرين فقط، أو من جميع أهل الدنيا، أو المعتبر مرتبة متوسطة. وإبطال هذه الثلاثة بمثل الكلام الذي ذكرناه في الوجه المتقدم: معلوم. والوجه الثالث: إن العدم نفي محض، فيمتنع أن يكون امتيازاً عن غيره بوجه من الوجوه. وإذا لم يحصل فيه الامتياز، امتنع كونه دليلا، ولا جزء دليل. لأن أقل مراتب الدليل المخصوص، امتيازه عما سواه. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: إن دلالة المعجز على الصدق غير مشروطة بعدم المعارضة. فهذا القسم ظاهر الفساد والبطلان. فثبت بما ذكرنا: فساد القسمين، وثبت بفسادهما: أن المعجز لا يمكن أن يكون دالاً على الصدق.
The third doubt: If the indication of the miracle (muʿjiz) to truthfulness occurred, it would either be conditioned by the absence of opposition (musharṭa bi-ʿadam al-muʿāraḍa) or not conditioned by it; both divisions are invalid, so the claim that a miracle indicates truthfulness is invalid. For reasons: We say: It cannot be that this indication is conditioned by the absence of opposition. First: Either the absence of opposition at the moment is sufficient for the act to be a miracle, or the absence of opposition forever is considered, [or the absence of opposition in an intermediate rank between the two mentioned ranks; and the three divisions] are false. As for the absence of opposition at the moment: it is not sufficient for the act to be a miracle. How many a man performs an act, and those present at the moment are unable to oppose it, yet that act is not considered a miracle by consensus. As for the second division: that the condition for it being a miracle is the absence of opposition forever; this condition is unknown. Who can know that no one of those who come after him until the Day of Resurrection can produce an opposition to it? And when this condition becomes unknown, the conditioned also becomes unknown, so it follows that all miracles become unknown. As for the third division: which is the intermediate rank between the two mentioned ranks. We say: Those intermediate ranks are many and varied. Considering some of them as preferable to considering the others is pure arbitrariness, and that is invalid. [So it is established that conditioning the absence of opposition is divided into these three divisions, and it is established that all of them are false divisions, so the claim of considering the absence of opposition is false.] The second argument for the corruption of this division: The considered absence of opposition is either from those present only, or from all the people of the world, or an intermediate rank is considered. And refuting these three with the same discussion mentioned in the previous argument is clear. The third argument: Non-existence (ʿadam) is pure negation (nafy maḥḍ), so it cannot be a differentiator from others in any way. And when it has no differentiation, it cannot be a proof, nor even part of a proof, because the minimum level of a specific proof is its differentiation from what is other than it. As for the second division: that it is said that the indication of the miracle to truthfulness is not conditioned by the absence of opposition; this division is clearly false and invalid. So it is established by what we have mentioned: the corruption of both divisions, and by their corruption it is established that the miracle cannot indicate truthfulness.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection (shubha) against miracles indicating truth and refutes it in detail. The objection states that if a miracle could indicate truth, that indication must either be conditioned by the absence of any opposing act (muʿāraḍa) or not conditioned; both options are argued to be false. The author refutes first the case where it is conditioned. He divides this into three possibilities: (1) absence of opposition at the moment, (2) absence forever, (3) an intermediate rank. All three are refuted: (1) momentary absence does not distinguish miracles since many non-miraculous acts lack opposition temporarily; (2) eternal absence is unknowable, so miracles would become unknown; (3) intermediate ranks are arbitrary. Then he gives two additional arguments. The second argument considers the scope of absence: only present people, all people of the world, or an intermediate scope—these are refuted similarly. The third argument: non-existence (ʿadam) is pure negation and cannot serve as a distinguishing feature for a proof. Finally, the second principal division (indication not conditioned) is declared obviously false. Thus both divisions are invalid, so miracles cannot indicate truth.

Key Terms
معجز
معارضة
مشروطة
مجهول
امتياز
عدم
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the absence of opposition is a clear and testable condition.
Correction: Actually it is either insufficient (temporary absence) or unknowable (eternal absence) or arbitrary (intermediate ranks), so it cannot serve as a valid condition for a miracle.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the condition of 'absence of opposition forever' invalid?
A) It is impossible to know if anyone in the future will oppose it.
B) It is too strict.
C) It does not apply to all miracles.
D) It contradicts the definition of a miracle.

Key Takeaway
The condition of no opposition is not a valid basis for a miracle's truth indication because it is either insufficient, unknowable, or arbitrary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, when you evaluate whether something is true—like a claim from a person or a teaching—do you often rely on the absence of immediate evidence against it? How has that superficial standard led you to be convinced of things that later turned out false? What would it take for you to adopt a deeper, more rigorous standard of truth?
?
Think of a belief you hold strongly about your purpose or destiny. Have you ever considered that your certainty might be based on an unknowable condition—like assuming no future event will challenge it? Are you willing to examine that belief with the same logical rigor this lesson applies to miracles? If not, what are you afraid of finding?
?
This lesson shows that an arbitrary condition cannot justify a proof. Is there an area of your life where you demand a specific condition to be met before you act, but that condition is actually arbitrary or based on personal preference? How is that condition keeping you from moving forward in faith or action? Commit to one step to test that condition today.

The Fourth Objection: Miracles Are Inappropriate Evidence
Author presents the fourth objection using an analogy with geometry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against miracles as evidence for prophethood and how it uses the concept of appropriateness.

Core Idea
Evidence must be appropriate to the claim it supports.

The objection argues that miracles are not suitable evidence for prophethood because they do not directly address the prophet's core message of guiding to the beginning and return. Using the analogy of a geometry scholar, the author illustrates that evidence must match the claim. This lesson unpacks the objection, allowing you to see its logic before the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: statement of objection, then an analogy, then application to prophethood.
The Arabic term 'ghayr munāsiba' (inappropriate) is central.
2
Identify the objection's claim
The objection claims that a miracle is an inappropriate (ghayr munāsiba) indication for truthfulness. Understand what 'appropriate' means here: the evidence should directly relate to the claim.
3
Analyze the geometry analogy
Compare the geometry scholar who must demonstrate problems to prove knowledge, versus performing an unrelated act like fasting. Why is fasting not appropriate? Because it doesn't show geometry knowledge.
Think about what would be appropriate for a prophet.
4
Connect to prophethood
The objection says a prophet's mission is to guide to the beginning and return. A miracle that is not directly about this guidance would be inappropriate. See how the analogy maps onto the prophethood claim.
This sets up the need for a refutation.
5
Reflect on the key idea
The core idea is that evidence must be relevant (munāsib) to the claim. This objection forces us to think about what makes a miracle proper evidence for prophethood.
Next lessons will show how the author refutes this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة: قالوا دلالة المعجز على الصدق دلالة غير مناسبة للمطلوب، فكانت باطلة ومثاله: أن الرجل إذا قال: إن عالم بالهندسة. فإذا طالبوه ببيان المسائل الهندسية، فإنه لا يأتي بها، ولا يشتغل بالشروع في شرح تلك المسائل، بل يقول: الدليل على أني عالم بهذا العلم: أني امتنع عن الأكل والشرب عشرين يوماً مع أني لا أموت. فإن كل أحد يقول: هذا الدليل لا يناسب هذا المطلوب، بل يجب أن يبين علمه بالهندسة، بأن يشرع في تلك المسائل ويشرحها على الوجه. فكذا ههنا الرسول هو الذي يرشد الخلق إلى معرفة المبدأ والمعاد، ويهديهم إلى طرق اكتساب المصالح في الدنيا وفي الآخرة.
The fourth doubt: They said: The indication of the miracle (muʿjiz) to truthfulness is an indication inappropriate (ghayr munāsiba) to the desired matter (maṭlūb), so it is invalid. Its example: that if a man says: 'I am a scholar of geometry,' and they ask him to explain geometrical problems, he does not produce them and does not engage in explaining those problems, but says: 'The proof that I am a scholar of this science is that I refrain from eating and drinking for twenty days while I do not die.' Then everyone would say: 'This proof is not appropriate to this desired matter; rather, he must show his knowledge of geometry by starting those problems and explaining them properly.' Likewise here, the messenger (rasūl) is the one who guides the creation to the knowledge of the beginning (mabdaʾ) and the return (maʿād), and guides them to the ways of acquiring benefits in this world and the Hereafter.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection (shubha) against miracles as evidence for prophethood. The objection argues that a miracle is an inappropriate (ghayr munāsib) indication of truthfulness because it does not match the intended purpose. The author provides an analogy: if someone claims to be a geometry scholar, the appropriate proof would be to demonstrate geometry problems, not to perform an unrelated miraculous act like fasting for twenty days. Similarly, the prophet's mission is to guide people to knowledge of the beginning (mabdaʾ) and the return (maʿād) and to show paths to benefit in this life and the hereafter. Therefore, a miracle that is not directly related to this guidance would be an inappropriate proof. The objection implies that miracles are not suitable evidence for the truth of prophethood because they do not directly address the core message.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are completely irrelevant to prophethood and cannot serve as evidence.
Correction: The objection does not deny miracles altogether; it claims they are not appropriate (ghayr munāsiba) evidence for the specific claim of prophethood, because they do not directly address the message. The appropriateness criterion is the focus.

Checkpoint

A) Miracles are impossible.
B) Miracles are inappropriate as evidence for the truth of prophethood.

Key Takeaway
The objection argues that evidence must be appropriate to the claim, but it will be refuted by showing that miracles are indeed appropriate signs of divine truth.

But are you sometimes like them, waiting for God to speak in a specific format while ignoring the miracles already unfolding in your life? Write down one 'inappropriate' blessing you received this week that you brushed aside.
?

Would it be 'appropriate'—directly connected to following the Prophet's guidance—or would you rely on miraculous stories that don't change how you live? Commit to one action today that makes your evidence match your claim.

The Fourth Doubt: Miracles Lack Suitability to the Claim
Presents the objection that miracles are not suitable evidence for prophethood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth doubt against prophetic miracles that argues miracles are not suitable evidence for prophethood due to lack of logical connection.

Core Idea
Miracles must be relevant to the claim to prove it.

The author presents the fourth doubt raised by skeptics. They argue that miracles do not have the appropriate connection (munāsaba) to the claim of prophethood. Using a clear analogy, they explain why miracles like turning a staff into a snake are not considered proper evidence for guiding people to the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to capture the argument's flow.
This is an opponent's objection, not the author's own view.
2
Understand the analogy
The geometry claimant analogy is key: a person claiming geometry cannot prove it by fasting. Identify how this mirrors the prophethood claim.
3
Identify the core argument
The doubt claims miracles are unsuitable because they are not directly related to the prophetic mission of guidance. The evidence must be appropriate (munāsib) to the claim.
4
Note the author's stance
Remember that the author is presenting this doubt for later refutation. Do not assume he endorses it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : قالوا دلالة المعجز على الصدق دلالة غير مناسبة للمطلوب، فكانت باطلة ومثاله : أن الرجل إذا قال: إن عالم بالهندسة . فإذا طالبوه ببيان المسائل الهندسية ، فإنه لا يأتي بها ، ولا يشتغل بالشروع في شرح تلك المسائل ، بل يقول : الدليل على أني عالم بهذا العلم : أني امتنع عن الأكل والشرب عشرين يوماً مع أني لا أموت . فإن كل أحد يقول : هذا الدليل لا يناسب هذا المطلوب ، بل يجب أن يبين علمه بالهندسة ، بأن يشرع في تلك المسائل ويشرحها على الوجه. فكذا ههنا الرسول هو الذي يرشد الخلق إلى معرفة المبدأ والمعاد ، ويهديهم إلى طرق اكتساب المصالح في الدنيا وفي الآخرة، فوجب أن يبين هذه الدعوى بإظهار هذا المعنى. فأما أن يقول : الدليل على أني كذلك ، أني أقلب العصا ثعباناً ، فهذا الذليل لا يناسب هذا المطلوب ، فوجب أن لا يلتفت إليه .
The fourth doubt: They said: the indication of the miracle upon truthfulness is an unsuitable indication for the sought matter, so it is invalid. And its example: that if a man says: I am knowledgeable in geometry. And if they demand from him the demonstration of geometrical problems, then he does not produce them, nor does he busy himself with starting to explain those problems, but rather he says: the proof that I am knowledgeable in this science is that I refrain from eating and drinking for twenty days while I do not die. Then every person says: this proof is not suitable for this sought matter, rather he must demonstrate his knowledge of geometry by starting in those problems and explaining them properly. So likewise here, the messenger is the one who guides the creation to the knowledge of the beginning and the return, and guides them to the ways of acquiring benefits in this world and the Hereafter, so it is necessary that he demonstrates this claim by manifesting this meaning. But as for saying: the proof that I am like that is that I turn the staff into a snake, so this proof is not suitable for this sought matter, so it is necessary not to pay attention to it.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth doubt against the evidential value of miracles. The objection is that miracles lack munāsaba (suitability/appropriateness) to the claim of prophethood. The example clarifies: a person claiming knowledge of geometry cannot prove it by fasting; he must demonstrate geometrical knowledge. Similarly, a prophet who claims to guide people to the afterlife and worldly benefits should prove his claim through matters relevant to that guidance, not through turning a stick into a snake. Since miracles are not directly suited to the claimed message, they should be disregarded as evidence. The author does not respond here; this is the exposition of the doubt.

First Objection from Determinism Against Miracles as Proof
First determinist objection against miracles as proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the determinist argument that if God creates all human actions, He could also create a miracle that misleads people, thus undermining the evidentiary value of miracles.

Core Idea
If God creates all actions, He can create both ignorance and misleading speech.

The first objection to miracles as proofs comes from the doctrine of determinism (jabr). Deniers argue that if God creates every human action, then He also creates lies and ignorance. Therefore, He could create a miracle that misleads people, making miracles unreliable as signs of truthfulness. This lesson examines this objection in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the garbled parts due to OCR but focus on the argument.
The passage contains the first of four doubts (shubahat) from deniers.
2
Identify the logical steps
Trace the argument: from determinism to God creating all actions, to creating lies and ignorance, to the possibility of creating misleading speech.
The argument uses a comparison: creating misleading speech is not worse than creating ignorance directly.
3
Reflect on the underlying premise
Consider what the objection assumes about God's power and action. How does this challenge the reliability of miracles?
The objection does not deny God's power but uses it to cast doubt on miracles as proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الساخر في أن بتقدير أن يكون المعية الانبا سلام ما لخا صدق الله تعالى على سبيل التصيح. فضل يلزم من هذا كون المدعي صاحقا؟ قال (۱) المنكرون لدلالة المعجزات : إن هذا المعنى غير واجب . ويدل الله عليه وجوه : الشبهة الأولى : [ إن الدلائل الدالة على (۲) ] صحة القول بالجبر ، دالة على أن فاعل جميع أفعال العباد هو الله تعالى . وإذا ثبت هذا ، وجب القطع بان خالق كل الأكاذيب ، وكل الجهالات هو الله تعالى ، وإذا لم يمتنع من تعالى خلق الجهل والضلالة ابتداء ، فبأن لا يمتنع منه ذكر كلام يوجب وقوع التلبيس والجهل والشبهة في قلب العباد ، كان أولى . لأن فعل ما قد يفضي إلى الجهل ، ليس بأعظم من فعل الجهل ابتداء .
The tenth chapter on that with the supposition that the miracle indicates the truth of God Almighty in the manner of correctness? So does it necessarily follow from this that the claimant is truthful? The deniers of the indication of miracles said: This meaning is not necessary. And God indicates it through aspects: The first doubt: [The proofs that indicate] the validity of the statement of determinism indicate that the doer of all actions of servants is God Almighty. And when this is established, it must be certain that the creator of all lies and all ignorances is God Almighty. And if it is not impossible for the Exalted to create ignorance and error initially, then that He does not refrain from mentioning speech that necessitates the occurrence of deception, ignorance, and doubt in the hearts of servants is more fitting. Because doing what may lead to ignorance is not greater than doing ignorance initially.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (shubha) from the deniers of miracles as proofs of truthfulness. The chapter heading is garbled but seems to be about whether the fact that a miracle occurs with a claimant necessarily proves his truthfulness. The deniers assert that this meaning (that miracles indicate truthfulness) is not necessary. They support this with four doubts (shubahat). The first doubt relies on the doctrine of determinism (jabr). The argument proceeds: The proofs for determinism show that God is the doer of all human actions. If so, He is the creator of all lies and all ignorance. Since God does not refrain from creating ignorance and error directly, it is even more fitting that He does not refrain from uttering speech that causes deception, ignorance, and doubt in people's hearts. This is because causing something that leads to ignorance is not greater than causing ignorance itself. In other words, if God can directly create ignorance (which is a greater evil), then He can certainly create speech that leads to ignorance (a lesser evil). Thus, the objection aims to show that God could support a liar with miracles, so miracles are not necessarily signs of truthfulness.

Key Terms
المعجزات
الجبر
الجهل
التلبيس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles always prove the truth of the claimant because God would never support a liar.
Correction: This objection argues that if God is the creator of all actions, He could create a miracle to support a liar, so miracles are not automatically proofs.

Checkpoint

A) God is all-powerful and can do anything.
B) God creates all actions, including lies and ignorance.
C) Miracles are not under God's control.
D) The claimant might be truthful after all.

Key Takeaway
The determinist objection challenges the evidentiary value of miracles by arguing that God could create misleading signs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If it does, how do you reconcile it with your trust in the signs Allah has sent? Write down one concrete reason from your study of prophethood that dispels this doubt for you.

Reflect on a time when a doubt about your faith made you uneasy. What will you do the next time you encounter a philosophical challenge to Islamic beliefs? Commit to one action, such as studying the refutation or asking a knowledgeable person.

The Second Objection: Infinite Regress of Ignorance
Deniers argue that since ignorance requires an uncaused first ignorance, God must be its creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the deniers argue that ignorance in human hearts must be caused by God, and why this forces the conclusion that God can also create miracles.

Core Idea
Ignorance cannot be self-caused; it must originate from an external source, namely God.

The deniers present a clever argument to show that God is the creator of ignorance. They start with the undeniable existence of ignorance in people's hearts and then ask who causes it. By eliminating the possibility that the servant causes his own ignorance, they force the conclusion that God is the cause. This argument also serves to support the idea that God can create miracles, since creating ignorance is an even greater departure from truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Possibilities
Read the first part of the passage where the deniers state that ignorance exists, and the doer is either the servant or God. Note the dichotomy.
This sets up the entire argument.
2
Examine the First Possibility (Servant)
The passage breaks the servant's possible causation into two subcases: either he knowingly seeks ignorance, or he mistakes ignorance for knowledge. Understand why each subcase fails.
Focus on the logical reasoning for the first subcase being impossible.
3
Analyze the Infinite Regress
The second subcase leads to an infinite regress of ignorance. Work through the logic: each instance of ignorance is explained by a prior ignorance, which itself requires explanation, ad infinitum.
The impossibility of infinite regress is key to the argument.
4
Conclude Divine Causation
The regress forces a first ignorance that the servant did not intend, so God must be its creator. Then note the final implication that God can create speech that suggests ignorance (i.e., miracles).
This conclusion ties the objection to the broader debate on miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : لاشك في حصول الجهالات في قلوب الخلق . ففاعل هذا الجهل. إما أن يكون هو العبد ، أو الله تعالى . والأول باطل . لأنه إما أن يقال : العبد رغب في تحصيل الجهل لنفسه ابتداء مع علمه بكونه جهلا . أو يقال : العبد إنما رغب في تحصيل ذلك [ الجهل ، لأنه اعتقد فيه كونه علماً ، فلاجل حصول هذا الاشتباه ، رغب في تحصيل ذلك (۳) ] الاعتقاد لنفسه . والأول باطل من وجهين : أحدهما : إن من المعلوم بالضرورة : أن العاقل لا يسعى في تحصيل الجهل لنفسه . والثاني : [ إنه متى علم كونه جهلاً ، فإنه مع هذا العلم ، يمتنع ، يمتنع ان يصير جاهلاً بذلك الشيء . فيثبت : أن هذا القسم فاسد . وأن الحق هو القسم الثاني ، وهو أن العبد إنما رغب في تحصيل ذلك الاعتقاد (۱) ] لنفسه ، لأنه اعتقد أن ذلك الاعتقاد : علم . فعلى هذا إنما رغب في تحصيل هذا الاعتقا: لأجل الجهل السابق . فنعيد التقسيم في ذلك الجهل السابق . فإن كان ذلك لأجل آخر يتقدمه ، لزم منه التسلسل ، وهو محال . فثبت : أن هذه الجهالات تترقى عند التصاعد إلى جهل أول ، وقع في القلب [ ومعلوم أن العبد لم يقصد إيقاعه ، فوجب أن يكون وقوعه في القلب (۳) ] لأجل أن الله تعالى خلقه فيه فثبت : أن خالق كل الجهالات في القلوب هو الله تعالى . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فبأن يجوز كونه فاعلاً لما يوهم الجهل ، كان أولى
The second doubt: There is no doubt about the occurrence of ignorances in the hearts of people. So the doer of this ignorance is either the servant or God Almighty. The first is false. Because either it is said: The servant desired to acquire ignorance for himself initially while knowing it to be ignorance. Or it is said: The servant only desired to acquire that [ignorance, because he believed it to be knowledge; due to this confusion, he desired to acquire that] belief for himself. The first is false in two ways: One: It is known necessarily that a reasonable person does not strive to acquire ignorance for himself. The second: [When he knows it to be ignorance, then with this knowledge, it is impossible for him to become ignorant of that thing. So it is established that this section is corrupt. And the truth is the second section, which is that the servant only desired to acquire that belief] for himself, because he believed that belief to be knowledge. So on this basis, he only desired to acquire this belief because of prior ignorance. So we repeat the division concerning that prior ignorance. If that was for another preceding it, then an infinite regress follows, which is impossible. So it is established that these ignorances ascend to a first ignorance that occurred in the heart [and it is known that the servant did not intend to cause it, so it is necessary that its occurrence in the heart] is because God Almighty created it in him. So it is established that the creator of all ignorances in the hearts is God Almighty. And when this is established, then that it is permissible for Him to be the doer of what suggests ignorance is more fitting.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection from the deniers. It begins by noting that ignorance exists in people's hearts. The question is: who causes that ignorance? Either the servant himself or God. The deniers argue that the servant cannot be the cause, for two reasons. First, if the servant knowingly seeks ignorance, that is impossible because a rational person does not knowingly seek ignorance. Second, if the servant believes the ignorance is knowledge, then he is actually seeking what he thinks is knowledge, not ignorance. That leads to an infinite regress: every instance of ignorance is explained by an earlier ignorance, which itself must be explained by an earlier one, and so on. This regress is impossible. Therefore, the only alternative is that the first ignorance is caused by God, not the servant. Consequently, God is the creator of all ignorance. If He can create ignorance itself (a greater evil), then it is even more fitting that He can create speech that suggests ignorance (a lesser evil). Thus, miracles are not necessarily signs of truthfulness.

Key Terms
الجهل
التسلسل
الجهل الأول
القسمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes ignorance is always harmful to the servant.
Correction: The argument does not rely on harm; it focuses on the logical impossibility of self-caused ignorance without prior ignorance, regardless of harm.

Checkpoint
Why does the objection claim that the servant cannot cause his own ignorance?
A) Because ignorance is always a punishment from God.
B) Because the servant either knows it is ignorance (and won't seek it) or mistakes it for knowledge (which requires prior ignorance).
C) Because the servant has free will.
D) Because ignorance is a privation.

Key Takeaway
The existence of ignorance requires a first cause outside human will, which must be God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief that turns out to be false, do you immediately blame your own ignorance, or do you consider that your very first ignorance is from God, demanding you to seek His guidance? What does this realization require of you today?
?
The argument shows that a rational person does not knowingly seek ignorance. In what areas of your life do you settle for ignorance rather than seeking truth, knowing that this ignorance may have a divine origin? What is one specific step you will take to overcome that ignorance?
?
If God is the creator of all ignorances, does that make you passive in your pursuit of knowledge? Or does it drive you to rely on Him more earnestly? What would change in your daily habits of learning if you truly believed that your ignorance is not merely your fault but a trial from God?

Fourth Objection Begins: God Cannot Support a Liar
Opening of the fourth objection, stating its central thesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the starting point of the fourth objection against prophethood and its claim about the impossibility of God supporting a liar.

Core Idea
The fourth objection argues that God cannot support a liar with miracles.

The fourth objection begins by stating its central claim: that it is impossible for God to support a liar. This lesson will examine the opening sentence and set the stage for the full argument. Although the text cuts off, we can already identify the key issue at stake.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence once and its English translation. Notice the precise wording: 'تصديق الكاذب محال على الله'.
The term 'محال' implies impossibility, but the fuller theological context is about what is befitting for God.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on 'تصديق الكاذب' (supporting a liar) and 'محال' (impossible). These are the core concepts of the objection.
3
Consider the implication
Think about why someone would claim that supporting a liar is impossible for God. How does this relate to the proof of prophethood through miracles?
This objection challenges the standard argument that miracles confirm a prophet's truthfulness.
4
Understand the context
Recognize that this is the fourth of several objections. The full argument is missing from the text, but we can infer the direction from the opening.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : مدار كلام القائلين بأن تصديق الكاذب محال على الله .
The fourth doubt: The pivot of the statement of those who say that supporting a liar is impossible for God.

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the fourth objection. It states that the core of the argument of those who claim that supporting a liar (i.e., giving miracles to a false prophet) is impossible for God is centered on a particular point. Unfortunately, the text cuts off here, so the full argument is not available. The phrase 'مدار كلام القائلين' (the pivot of the statement of those who say) indicates that the objection will address the basis of the opposing position and refute it. Without the rest, we cannot analyze the argument further.

Key Terms
تصديق الكاذب
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the fourth objection is about God's power being limited.
Correction: The objection is about God's wisdom and justice, not His power. It claims it would be unbecoming for God to support a liar, not that He cannot do so.

Checkpoint

A) That miracles are impossible.

C) That prophethood is unnecessary.
D) That God cannot create miracles.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection opens by asserting that God cannot support a liar, a key premise in the debate on prophetic miracles.

The First Doubt Against Tawatur: False Tawatur Reports
Objectors raise the first doubt that tawatur reports can be false, thus not yielding certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first doubt raised against the certainty of tawatur reports and why opponents argue that tawatur does not guarantee truth.

Core Idea
Tawatur reports can be false? The first doubt challenges their certainty.

Opponents of prophetic miracles argue that they have not personally witnessed miracles, but only heard about them through chains of transmission. They raise a specific doubt: there are many reports that meet the criteria of tawatur yet are considered false by believers. This casts doubt on the claim that tawatur always yields certain knowledge. Let's examine this doubt as presented in the source.

Guided Path
1
Read the introduction to the doubt
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the opponents preface their doubt by stating they have not seen miracles themselves, but only heard of them through chains.
The phrase 'إنا ما رأينا شيئاً من هذه المعجزات' sets the scene.
2
Identify the specific doubt
Find the sentence: 'إن خبر التواتر حاصل في صور كثيرة، مع أنكم تحكمون بكونها كذباً'. This is the core of the first doubt: tawatur reports exist in many cases, yet you (the believers) judge them to be false.
This directly challenges the claim that tawatur produces certainty.
3
Understand the reasoning
Ask yourself: If tawatur is supposed to guarantee truth, how can there be false tawatur reports? The objectors use this as evidence that tawatur does not always yield knowledge.
Note that the author will later respond to this doubt.
4
Consider the implications
Reflect on why this doubt is significant. If tawatur can be false, then the transmission of miracles through tawatur is not reliable, undermining the proof of prophethood.
This connects to broader arguments about the validity of transmitted knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث عشر في الطعن في التواتر. قالوا : إنا ما رأينا شيئاً من هذه المعجزات . ولكننا سمعنا من جماعة أنهم قالوا : سمعنا من أقوام آخرين . وهكذا ، على هذا الترتيب إلى أن اتصل هذا الخبر بأقوام ، زعموا : أنهم شاهدوا هذه المعجزات . ونحن لا نسلم أن مثل هذا الخبر يفيد اليقين التام. والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الشبهة الأولى : إن خبر التواتر حاصل في صور كثيرة ، مع أنكم تحكمون بكونها كذباً . وذلك يقدح في كون التواتر مفيداً للعلم .
Chapter Thirteen: On Attacking Tawatur. They said: We have not seen any of these miracles. But we heard from a group that they said: We heard from other peoples. And so on, in this order, until this report reached peoples who claimed that they witnessed these miracles. And we do not accept that such a report yields complete certainty. What indicates that are several points: The First Doubt: Indeed, a tawatur report occurs in many cases, while you judge it to be false. And that impugns tawatur being beneficial for knowledge.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the first doubt raised by opponents against the validity of tawatur (uninterrupted transmission) as a source of certainty. The doubters argue that they have not personally seen the miracles claimed by prophets, but only heard about them through chains of transmission. They do not accept that such transmitted reports give certain knowledge. Specifically, they point out that there are many instances where reports that meet the criteria of tawatur (i.e., reported by a large number at each level such that collusion on a lie is impossible) are actually false according to the believers themselves. This, they claim, undermines the claim that tawatur always produces certain knowledge. The author will presumably respond to this doubt later.

Key Terms
تواتر (tawatur)
خبر (report)
يقين (certainty)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that tawatur refers to any widespread report, but in kalam it has specific conditions that must be met.
Correction: Tawatur in kalam has strict conditions, such as a large number of reporters at each level, that make collusion on a lie impossible. Not every widespread report qualifies.

Checkpoint

A) Because they have not seen miracles themselves.
B) Because there are many cases of tawatur reports that are false.
C) Because the chains of transmission are broken.
D) Because the reports are from different religions.

Key Takeaway
The first doubt against tawatur argues that because false tawatur reports exist, tawatur cannot guarantee certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponents base their doubt on not having seen miracles personally. Reflect: Is your own faith in the Prophet ﷺ built on personal experience or on the testimony of others? Have you ever critically examined the chain of transmission that brings you this knowledge? Are you willing to move from inherited belief to conviction, or are you content with hearsay? This is not a comfortable question — it demands you to own your faith.
?
The first doubt suggests that because false reports exist, all tawatur is unreliable. Consider: Are there truths you have rejected because you found a counterfeit? In your spiritual life, do you allow the existence of hypocrites to shake your trust in sincere believers? This lesson challenges you to not let the false invalidate the true. Have you done the work to discern genuine tawatur from falsehood? If not, what is stopping you?
?
The opponents say 'We do not accept that such a report yields complete certainty.' Ask yourself: What is the basis of your certainty in the foundational matters of your deen? Is it blind imitation or verified knowledge? If someone asked you tomorrow why you believe in the prophethood of Muhammad ﷺ, would you be able to present a convincing proof from tawatur? This lesson is a call to intellectual responsibility. Will you answer it?

The First Objection Against Tawatur: False Reports Also Spread Widely
Opponents deny tawatur gives certainty and present the first objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection that opponents of tawatur raise, specifically that tawatur exists in cases considered false, thus undermining its claim to certainty.

Core Idea
Opponents argue tawatur fails because false reports also spread widely.

The reliability of mass transmission (tawatur) is central to establishing prophetic miracles. Opponents of tawatur argue that simply because a report is widespread does not guarantee its truth. They present a series of doubts; the first is that tawatur itself occurs in many cases that are known to be false. This challenge forces us to examine the conditions under which tawatur yields certainty.

The objection is presented as the first of multiple aspects (وجوه).
3
Understand the core challenge
The opponents assert that tawatur is not a reliable source of knowledge because it includes cases that Muslims themselves reject as false. This is a direct attack on the principle.
Think of examples like widespread rumors that are false.

If tawatur cannot be trusted, then the reports of miracles are not certain, weakening the proof of prophethood.

The Jewish Report Dilemma: Challenging Tawatur
First example: Jewish report on eternal law and Sabbath, Muslims reject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first example used to argue that tawatur can convey falsehood, and the logical dilemma it presents.

Core Idea
A tawatur report that contradicts Islamic belief forces a choice: either the report is false, or its content is false.

This lesson explores a classic example where a widely transmitted report (tawatur) is claimed to be false. The example involves Jewish reports about the eternity of the Mosaic law and the Sabbath. The author frames it as a dilemma: if the report is true, then Islam's claim of abrogation is false; if the report is false, then the tawatur that conveys it is unreliable. This sets the stage for discussing how tawatur can be false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the careful structure: first the claim of tawatur, then the content of the report, then the dilemma.
The passage uses conditional reasoning: 'If ... then ...'
2
Identify the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities: either the report is true or false. Trace the consequences of each horn. Write them down in your own words.
3
Reflect on the challenge to tawatur
Think about why this example is problematic for the reliability of tawatur. How would you respond if someone used this argument?
Consider that Muslims believe the Jewish law was abrogated by Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقام الأول في بيان أن التواتر قد حصل في صور، اتفق المسلمون بها على كونه باطلاً، فتقريره من وجوه: الأول : إن اليهود على كثرتهم وتفرقهم في مشارق الأرض ومغاربها، يخبرون عن موسى عليه السلام أنه قال: إن شريعتي باقية وأنها لا تصير منسوخة البتة. وأنه عليه السلام قال : « عليكم بالسبت ما دامت السموات والأرض، فهذا الخبر إما أن يكون صدقاً أو كذباً. فإن كان صدقاً، فقد بطلت شريعتكم، وإن كان كذباً، فقد صار هذا التواتر باطلاً.
As for the first station in explaining that tawatur has occurred in cases that Muslims agree are false, its exposition is through aspects: The first: Indeed the Jews, despite their multitude and dispersion in the east and west of the earth, report about Moses, peace be upon him, that he said: 'My law is eternal and it will never become abrogated,' and that he, peace be upon him, said: 'You must keep the Sabbath as long as the heavens and the earth [endure].' So this report is either true or false. If it is true, then your law is invalidated; and if it is false, then this tawatur becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents the first example of a tawatur claim that Muslims reject. The opponents argue that Jews, despite their vast numbers and wide dispersal, unanimously report that Moses declared his law eternal and the Sabbath perpetual. Muslims, however, believe that Moses' law is abrogated by Islam and that the Sabbath is no longer binding. The argument is a dilemma: if the Jewish transmission is true, then Islam's claim to abrogate is false; if it is false, then the tawatur that conveys it is unreliable. Thus, this case shows tawatur can be false.

Key Terms
تواتر (tawatur)
منسوخة (abrogated)
السبت (Sabbath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This example is a refutation of Islam itself.
Correction: It is actually a challenge to the reliability of tawatur, not a direct refutation of Islam. Muslims can respond by denying the authenticity of the report or by arguing that tawatur can be false in cases of contradiction with established truth.

Checkpoint

A) If Jewish tawatur is true, Islam is false; if false, tawatur is invalid.
B) The Sabbath is eternal.
C) Muslims agree with Jews.
D) Tawatur is always true.

Key Takeaway
The first example of a false tawatur uses a dilemma: if the Jewish report is true, Islam is false; if false, tawatur is unreliable, showing that tawatur can be false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Core Idea
Tawatur can convey widely accepted claims that are still rejected by another group.

This brief passage presents a concrete historical example where two communities both claim tawatur but disagree on the truth. It highlights that tawatur does not automatically guarantee acceptance across groups.

Third Example: Jewish-Christian Consensus on Crucifixion
Sanusi's third counter-example to tawatur reliability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Jewish-Christian consensus on the crucifixion is used as an example to challenge the reliability of tawatur (mass transmission).

Core Idea
Even unanimous reports can be false across different communities.

This lesson examines a concrete historical example that appears to undermine tawatur. The author presents the agreement of Jews and Christians on Jesus's crucifixion, and the Muslim denial, to argue that tawatur does not guarantee truth. This forces us to consider how conflicting tawatur reports should be evaluated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it presents a single short example as an objection.
The example is the third of several.
2
Identify the key claim

3
Understand the objection
If tawatur guaranteed truth, then both the Jewish-Christian report and the Muslim report would be true. But they contradict each other, so tawatur cannot be a sure path to knowledge.
This shows that tawatur alone does not determine truth.
4
Reflect on the significance
This example forces us to consider how to resolve conflicting tawatur reports, and whether additional criteria are needed to judge their truth.
